# Complete Guide to AWS Software Deployment

> Join IT architect and instructor Brandon Rich as you embark on a journey from cloud fundamentals to full DevOps automation, starting with EC2 essentials for standing up secure, scalable architecture and moving to infrastructure-as-code with CloudFormation before diving into AWS’s deep set of code deployment options. Discover how to rapidly build an app stack with Elastic Beanstalk; deploy, scale,

#### [What you should know](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/what-you-should-know?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] To get the most out of this course, there are a few things you should know. First, you should be comfortable with a web application framework, such as Ruby on Rails, [Python](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Python%20(Programming%20Language).md), [Django](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Django.md), or .NET. The AWS Services covered in this course can be used to deploy a wide selection of application types, but since we can't show every use case, we'll look at each AWS deployment option through the lens of a Ruby on Rails app running on Amazon [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md). If you're unfamiliar with Rails, fear not, the things you'll learn about Beanstalk, CodeDeploy, and the other AWS services will apply regardless of your preferred platform. If you plan on following along with the demos, it'll be helpful to have some familiarity with [Version Control](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Version%20Control.md) using [Git](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Git.md) as well as SSH, YAML, basic Shell scripting and [Web Servers](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Web%20Servers.md) such as NGINX. Remember, though, that the point of this course is learning AWS itself, so throughout the course I'll go into the details you need to know. You'll also want some basic fluency with the command line. For these sections, I'll be on a Mac terminal. If you're working on Linux, you'll be right at home. The AWS specific command line tools are cross platform, so [Windows](../../Glossary/Service/Windows.md) users should not see any variation there. However, you'll still need to know the Windows equivalent of Linux commands such as ls, cat, and SSH. Finally, there are a few things you'll need to have in advance of starting. First, Git command-line tools. You'll also need a text editor and an AWS account, which we'll discuss in the next video.

#### [Initial setup](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/initial-setup?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/initial-setup?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] If you want to create an AWS account to follow along with this course, it only takes a few steps. Just head to aws.[amazon.com](https://amazon.com) and click Create an AWS Account. Alternatively, you can click the Get started for free button and learn a little bit about the AWS Free Tier. If you're a first time AWS user, some of the resources we create during this course are eligible for the free tier. However, we will probably incur some charges. AWS instance time can be cheap, fractions of a cent per hour in some cases, but they can add up over time. To make sure you're not charged for anything beyond what you use in this course, make sure to watch the tear down videos at the end of each section. Still, I'd invite you to check out the AWS Free Tier documentation to understand the ways that you can maximize the opportunities to learn without costing anything. From here, click Create a Free Account. You'll head to this page which kicks off a short workflow where you give AWS details about yourself, set up a password, and give credit card information so AWS knows where to charge as you create resources that do cost money. Once you've activated your new account, you'll be ready to log in. You can always head back to aws.[amazon.com](https://amazon.com) and click the Sign In button here in the top. From here, you're given a choice, and you can sign in as the root user, which will be the account that you just created, if this is a new course, or an IAM user. The root user has full privileges on your account
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/initial-setup?u=76281980&t=94)** to create any kind of resource and manage billing information and all of the account level [Metadata](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Metadata.md). It's a very powerful account. And generally speaking, we don't want to use it as we do things over time. You'll see in this course, we'll occasionally create IAM users so that we can scope privileges down to just what we need an account to do for the given task. For now, we'll use the root login, and give our password. And we'll arrive at the AWS Console Home. Now, we'll explore this in detail in the next video, but first, I want to suggest one essential task in securing your account. We've just logged in as the root user. And even though it's great to use IAM users, if you're going to use root for anything, we should put multifactor authentication on it. Click into IAM, or search IAM in this top search bar, click IAM, and you are taken into the Identity and Access Management Dashboard. You'll have this option front and center to add MFA for the root user. Click Add MFA, and you'll be given instructions on how to set up an app like Duo or Okta Authenticate or [Google](../../Glossary/Service/Google.md) Authenticator to add a second factor to logging in with this root account. I highly recommend that you do it because that root account is a very powerful thing, and there are attackers out there that would just love to get ahold of it to spin up resources in your account to do things like mining [Cryptocurrency](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Cryptocurrency.md), whatever they may want to get up to.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/initial-setup?u=76281980&t=188)** So like any account that you hold at your bank or any other online account, adding MFA is a great step. So I suggest you do that. And then the next video, we'll explore the Home Management Console in even more detail.

This is going to be your new home for the duration of this course, and you're going to become very familiar with navigating it. Let me show you around a little bit. So what else is going on in this console? Well, one thing I really want to point out

> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/navigate-the-aws-management-console?u=76281980&t=94)** to you here is this dropdown. This is North Virginia, and if I click on it, it's going to show me all the available AWS regions. This is really important. What I want you to do in this course is to pick a region that's close to you and stick with it. You can see they've all got a location and they've all got a short name here. So North Virginia is US East 1. At various points in the course, we're going to need to put in this short name. So I'm going to stick with North Virginia, US East 1, you might want to do the same, but just pick one and stay with it the whole time. Also be aware, the resources that we build are often unique to a region. So if I build an EC2 instance in one region, it doesn't exist in other regions. One of the most sort of scary or startling things you can do as a new AWS user is to build a lot of resources, create some things that you need to use and then come into the console accidentally having selected a different region and all of your resources are gone. You are like, oh my gosh, everything's been deleted. Did I do something to accidentally, you know, kill those instances? No, they're still there. They're just in the original region and you've accidentally switched away from it. So keep an eye on that and make sure you know what region you're in at all times. Okay, what else is going on in this screen? Well, each of these boxes here, like recently visited, is called a widget and they can be moved around by grabbing these dots here and dragging and dropping in different arrangements. And you can click the three dots here and remove a widget outright. And if you accidentally do that for one that you didn't intend to, that's no problem.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/navigate-the-aws-management-console?u=76281980&t=186)** You can always go over here to add widgets and you'll see not only the widget that you deleted, but other ones that you can drag onto the screen. One that has probably come pre-configured on your new AWS account is cost and usage. This is very valuable because when you have this here, it's going to show you your current monthly spend on this account. It's always good in a cloud environment to know what you're spending on an ongoing basis because it can be easy to rack up charges if you're not careful. Throughout this course by the way, don't worry, at the end of every chapter, I include a tear down section so we make sure we delete everything that we stand up. But this can be really useful to always make sure every time you log into the console, you know what's going on with your spend. In fact, in the next video I'm going to show you how to set up a budget alarm on your account so that you're never surprised by your AWS bill.

#### [Set an AWS budget alarm](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/set-an-aws-budget-alarm?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/set-an-aws-budget-alarm?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] All right. In this video, we're going to set up a budgeting alarm in AWS, so that you'll always be aware when your spend starts to approach a threshold that you define, giving you time to take action. So we're going to go up here in the search bar and type Budgets. Now, budgets are a feature of a larger umbrella called Billing and [Cost Management](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Cost%20Management.md), so you see that reflected in the search results. We see the service here and the individual features below. Clicking Budgets will drill us into that section specifically. But if you came in through the top of Billing and cost Management, you've got this sidebar here, and you can always go directly to Budgets. Let's click Create budget. All right, we'll use a template that's preselected and click Monthly cost budget. We'll give the budget a name. I'll just call it MyBudget, but this could be more descriptive, because it is possible to set up multiple alarms and multiple thresholds. For here, I'll put in $50. I want to know whenever my spend starts to approach that in any given month. I'll give email recipients here. So this is where you put your own email address, so that you receive a notification. I'll just say a fake one here and move on. Here's the important bit in this window. You will be notified when your actual spend reaches 85% of this threshold, so 85% of 50, and it will notify you again when your actual spend reaches 100%, and it will alert you yet one more time
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/set-an-aws-budget-alarm?u=76281980&t=93)** if your forecasted spend is looking like it's going to hit 100% of this value. So you're going to get quite a bit of notification before you actually reach this spend amount. So this is a really useful thing to make sure that your costs never overrun. Click Create budget, and you're done. That's all that has to be done, except for monitoring your email address and watching for those emails to come in. So set that up at whatever dollar amount you're comfortable with, and that will really help you control your spend. Now, you might be wondering what happens when I get one of those emails, and that's where you're going to want to head over to a section called Cost Explorer. Now, there's a lot that could be said about how Cost Explorer works, but the main thing to know is you're going to get a graph that looks a little bit like this, and it will show you the ways that you're spending money in AWS. Each of these colors in this bar represents a different section of AWS. And so you can see this big blue section here is on [EC2](../../Skills/DevOps/Amazon%20EC2.md) instances. I'm spending some on RDS [Databases](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Databases.md). And so it shows you, and it shows you the breakdown of dollars and cents spent on each service. You can click into them and find a little bit more information, but that's a good indicator of where you need to go. You are the one creating these resources. So this should be enough of a hint to go, okay, there's a database I need to go shut down, or something like that. So with Cost Explorer and with Budget Setup, you should be in really good shape to know at all times how to manage your spend in the cloud.

### 1. AWS Elastic Compute Cloud (EC2) Foundations

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Understanding AWS EC2](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understanding-aws-ec2?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understanding-aws-ec2?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When we talk about compute resources in AWS, it all starts with Elastic Compute Cloud, or [EC2](../../Skills/DevOps/Amazon%20EC2.md). EC2 instances are [Virtual Machines](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Virtual%20Machines.md) that you launch in the AWS Cloud. They're created on demand or when combined with more advanced AWS services in response to conditions you define. EC2 instances use a pay-as-you-go pricing model. You only pay for the time your instances are running. The cost varies on the type of instance you create. For example, an instance with more RAM costs more to run. You may choose from many different operating systems, such as [Windows](../../Glossary/Service/Windows.md), [Ubuntu](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Ubuntu.md), [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md), Red Hat, and AWS's own Amazon Linux. The flexibility of the pay-as-you-go model and the ability to resize instances, combined with services that let you create and destroy instances when you need them, give you truly elastic capacity, hence the name Elastic Compute Cloud. One of the first choices you make when creating a new EC2 instance is what machine image to use as a foundation. Amazon Machine Images, or AMIs, are the molds from which new instances are built. They consist of basic installations of operating systems, like [Windows Server](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Windows%20Server.md) and [Red Hat Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Red%20Hat%20Linux.md). They may require you to have a license for the OS. And they can be customized for future use. Finally, you can create your own AMIs. So if you want to create an instance, install and configure the software you need, and then use that as the basis for future machine creation,
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understanding-aws-ec2?u=76281980&t=95)** you can. Creating an EC2 instance isn't like building a computer from scratch. Instead of deciding on the components, such as CPU, RAM, and Network Interfaces, AWS allows you to choose from a number of predefined [Hardware](../../Topics/Hardware.md) configurations. These are called instance types. Each type has a different on-demand hourly rate and they fall into several general categories or families. The families are as follows: general purpose, storage optimized, compute optimized, memory optimized, and accelerated computing, which is a grab bag for instance types that feature unique hardware, such as graphics processors or machine learning chips. Within a family, instance types have names, like T2 micro and M4 dot large, which are both members of the general purpose family. The T2 dot nano is the smallest machine you can provision, with one CPU and half a gig of RAM. The R3 dot large, belonging to the memory optimized family, gives you two virtual CPUs and 15 gigabytes of RAM. If you have the money, you can even provision the incredible X1 dot 32 X large, which boast 128 virtual CPUs and nearly two terabytes of RAM. T3 series, like the T2 machines before them, are the workhorses of AWS instance types. They're general purpose machines
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understanding-aws-ec2?u=76281980&t=190)** that come in a few different sizes, but who all share one characteristic: their CPUs are burstable, meaning you get extra CPU power when you need it. Your instance's ability to burst depends on a system of what AWS calls CPU credits, which you accrue over time during normal operation. You should also know that you can change an EC2 instances type at any time from the AWS console. All that takes is a simple restart. You can head to docs.aws.[amazon.com](https://amazon.com) for more details, but suffice it to say that a T3 micro is a fine place to start when provisioning a web server, small database, or a similar environment. When you create an EC2 instance, where is it actually physically located? Regions are geographical areas that are logically isolated from each other. You choose the region in which to launch your resources. In the United States, there are four regions: North California, or US West one, Oregon, also known as US West two, Virginia, called US East one, and the newest, Ohio, called US East two. There are also two special regions, GovCloud East and GovCloud West, that are used in cases where maintaining [Regulatory Compliance](../../Skills/Artificial%20Intelligence%20(AI)/Regulatory%20Compliance.md) requires additional security restrictions. A few of the others include Ireland, Frankfurt, Singapore, Seoul, Tokyo, Mumbai, and Sao Paulo. There are now also regions in Stockholm, Paris,
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understanding-aws-ec2?u=76281980&t=285)** Cape Town, South Africa, and Bahrain. Almost every resource you create in AWS is built within the context of a region. Though some resources are region-less, like S3, and very resilient designs consider spanning multiple regions, most individual resources you create will reside in just one. When selecting a region, you want to think about latency. Where will your customers be? Where will resources be that your instances need to connect to? This is especially important if your AWS instances will need to talk to hosts in an on-premises data center. A good rule of thumb is to pick the region closest to you, but be sure to measure. Depending on network topology, the closest physical data center might not give you the fastest round-trip packet time. Regions represent large geographical areas and they're logically isolated from each other. Within a region, AWS still gives you the option to geographically separate your resources by dividing regions into availability zones. Availability zones, or AZs, are the physical data centers that make up an AWS region. They're named by letter, so the first AZ in the US East one region is called US East 1B. Geographical separation is important when you need to build applications that must not go down, even if disaster strikes. Each AZ is a building located far enough apart that a physical threat to one AZ,
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understanding-aws-ec2?u=76281980&t=378)** such as fire or flood, will not affect the others, but close enough together so that network latency between them is negligible. This makes multi-AZ load balancing highly effective for building fault tolerance into your applications. What about storage? Though AWS does allow you to use a slice of the storage on the physical machines occupied by your EC2 VM, your starting point for storage should be a service called Elastic Block Store. EBS is a bit like an automatic hard drive installer. You specify what you'd like in terms of drive size, type, and volume mapping, and AWS handles everything for you. You can map EBS as one or multiple volumes, and conveniently, EBS lives independently of EC2, so you can reuse these drives with other instances. Unlike Instance Back Storage, which will not survive a reboot, EBS data lives on until you explicitly delete it. There are a host of other storage options provided by AWS for different use cases, including object storage, archival storage, and multi-user network storage. We go in depth on this topic, please see my other course: AWS Storage and [Data Management](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Data%20Management.md). As we continue using EC2, we'll learn even more: how to connect to instances using SSH and other methods, how to define [Network Security](../../Skills/Cybersecurity/Network%20Security.md) using [AWS Security](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/AWS%20Security.md) groups, and how to use tags to add [Metadata](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Metadata.md) to resources to help you track them better.
>
> **[7:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understanding-aws-ec2?u=76281980&t=470)** So those are the basics of EC2. We'll learn even more about this fundamental service as we delve into the AWS deployment and provisioning functions that build upon it.

#### [Understanding AWS load balancers](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understanding-aws-load-balancers?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understanding-aws-load-balancers?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's take a look at how AWS can distribute traffic across multiple instances using application or network load balancers. Benefits of AWS load balancers include high availability, fault tolerance, cross-zone load balancing, HTTP health checking, and integration with auto scaling groups. Imagine you have a web application on just one instance. The security group exposes web traffic ports to the world. When your app starts to receive more traffic than it can handle, you may wish to distribute the traffic across multiple copies of the web server. That's exactly what AWS's load balancing resources can do. A load balancer sits between your consumers and the web tier. Now, instead of making HTTP requests directly to an [EC2](../../Skills/DevOps/Amazon%20EC2.md) Instance, your users make requests to the address of the load balancer, which conveys those requests to the underlying resources. In terms of [Network Security](../../Skills/Cybersecurity/Network%20Security.md), the EC2 security groups should be configured to only allow web traffic from the load balancer. Meanwhile, the load balancer itself has a security group, which should be configured to expose your web traffic. The app still has a single interface or web address that of the load balancer now instead of the single EC2 Instance. But it has much more capacity on the backend. This picture depicts an elastic load balancer, the first generation of AWS load balancing technology. AWS has now re-dubbed elastic load balancers as classic
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understanding-aws-load-balancers?u=76281980&t=95)** and added to new options: the Network Load Balancer, or NLB, and the Application Load Balancer, or ALB. Let's take a look at how they work. One of the biggest differences between classic elastic load balancers and the newer generation options is the concept of target groups. Instead of connecting your instances directly to the load balancer, you instead define collections of hosts called target groups. A target group serves as a level of indirection between the load balancer and the underlying hosts. Target groups make a lot of sense when you consider the features an application load balancer brings over the classic ELB. Application load balancers listen for HTTP or HTTPS protocols on any port. They can integrate with AWS Certificate Manager to run TLS or HTTPS connections. And they operate on the request level and can make [Routing](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Routing.md) decisions based on HTTP headers. They can redirect requests, such as redirecting from unencrypted HTTP to encrypted HTTPS and can serve static content, such as maintenance notices or images. Application load balancers can support multiple target groups. With an ALB, you set routing rules. You might have one HTTPS listener and use your DNS system, such as Amazon's Route 53, and map two different application addresses to it. Then you can define rules on the listener
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understanding-aws-load-balancers?u=76281980&t=189)** that determine where the traffic goes. ALB's HTTP routing rules can be based on host header value or URL path pattern. In the previous example, you'd make your decision based on the host header. Each incoming URL would get routed to a different target group. Each target group has its own health check. Thus, this ALB essentially performs the work of two classic ELBs. How about network load balancers? NLBs operate on layer four. So they can't make routing decisions based on HTTP or URL values. You'll basically be mapping ports to ports. NLBs are simpler than ALBs, but they pack a lot of other benefits. For instance, they can handle millions of requests per second, can have multiple TCP port listeners, can be assigned a static IP address unlike ELBs or ALBs, and route requests to multiple ports on a single instance. If a health check fails, you'll want AWS to take that host out of the pool as soon as possible so users won't be routed to a failing host. With an NLB, you're required to have three consecutive fail checks before the host is considered unhealthy. Given that the minimum time between checks is 10 seconds, the lowest response time you can set as a fail is 6 seconds, you're looking at nearly 50 seconds of time a bad host might remain in the pool. It makes sense that AWS wants consecutive failures. After all, you wouldn't want to reduce the capacity just because of a transient network blip or slow response. Still, this behavior is something to keep in mind
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understanding-aws-load-balancers?u=76281980&t=283)** when designing your [System Architecture](../../Skills/Software%20Development/System%20Architecture.md). Earlier I mentioned one benefit of classic and application load balancers is integration with AWS Cert Manager for TLS termination. A secure web application should use HTTP over SSL, also known as HTTPS, to ensure that traffic sent to and from it is encrypted. At this point, I might have to explain something a bit confusing. SSL has been succeeded by a technology called TLS as the secure protocol of choice for the web. So these days, even though we still say HTTPS, what we really mean is HTTP over TLS. So from here on, I'll refer to TLS termination. TLS termination is the point at which traffic stops being encrypted and starts to be decrypted so that the application server can process it. In a single server scenario like this, you might install a TLS certificate on the server. Of course, your web app needs its requests in plain text, so the encrypted TLS traffic is terminated, that is, decrypted on the server itself, and your web app can process the request. Application load balancers let you install the TLS cert on the ALB itself through another AWS service called Certificate Manager. You can then have your instances listen on unencrypted ports. Traffic is encrypted up to the ALB, terminated by the ALB, and then it continues as plain text. If your instances are private, unencrypted traffic is never exposed.
>
> **[6:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understanding-aws-load-balancers?u=76281980&t=377)** You can upload your own TLS certificate, or with AWS Certificate Manager, AWS will issue one to your domain for free. Not only are they free, they're fully managed. AWS handles cert renewals for you automatically. Whether you're using Cert Manager or you've brought your own certificate, the next step is to enable a secure listener, such as HTTP port 443 on the ALB and associate the listener with the cert. Finally, a really nice aspect of this method is that if you deploy the same cert to multiple load balancers and you later need to update that certificate, you can do so from one place. Each ALB that uses the cert will be reconfigured at once.

#### [Understanding AWS Auto Scaling groups](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understanding-aws-auto-scaling-groups?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understanding-aws-auto-scaling-groups?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's take a look at AWS Auto Scaling. One of the main ways that AWS allows you to dynamically change your compute capacity. When you deploy a web application and you're taking it out of the cozy confines of your development environment and into the real world, where it will be exposed to real world traffic. As your app's popularity increases, so will its load. Eventually, your single instance will be overwhelmed as too many web requests max out its CPU, RAM, or network capacity. To solve this, you could just trade out your single host for a more powerful machine. That's called vertical scaling and an [EC2](../../Skills/DevOps/Amazon%20EC2.md), it'll require a reboot and some downtime while you change your instance type. Or you could introduce another host, then add a load balancer to distribute the traffic across these two hosts. This is called horizontal scaling, and it tends to be a more robust, resilient way of scaling an application, since, unlike with vertical scaling, you can add capacity without needing to reboot hosts. However, horizontal scaling can require some tedious manual work. You must build and provision new machines, deploy your application to them, add new machines to the load balancer, and monitor your application's load to know when to add instances and when it's safe to remove them. AWS auto scaling offers horizontal scaling as a service. It spins up new EC2 instances on demand or in response to conditions that you define.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understanding-aws-auto-scaling-groups?u=76281980&t=96)** It charges only for the AWS resources it creates for you. This service helps you achieve the elastic and elastic compute cloud, as your application, and therefore your cost, scale up and down based on your need. At the heart of AWS Auto Scaling is the Auto Scaling Group, or ASG. It's a logical container that holds your instances and decides when to add or remove from them. Load balancers are not part of auto scaling themselves, but can be pointed to ASGs to achieve horizontal auto scaling. Your ASG can receive notifications of policy events. These policies are expressed via CloudWatch alarms, meaning they can look for things like sustain high CPU usage or high demand network traffic. When the ASG receives a policy event, it can take the action that you prescribe to either scale up or scale down. The action then will be to either remove instances from your group or to add instances. If the ASG adds instances, they'll be built according to a launch template that you've defined. Launch templates are a blueprint for how a new instance should be built. They capture all the choices you'd make if you manually created an EC2 instance. You can then associate configuration with an ASG. If you've worked with EC2 directly, you should be familiar with some of the options you can set on a launch template, such as the AMI, the base image, such as Amazon, [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md),
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understanding-aws-auto-scaling-groups?u=76281980&t=192)** or [Windows Server](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Windows%20Server.md), the instance type such as t3.micro, security groups attached to each instance, the SSH key you'll use to log in, IAM instance roles, and storage options. Of course, a fresh EC2 instance in your ASG is no good, unless it's provisioned and running your app. What options do you have for making that happen? Well, you have a few choices. One is to prebake an AMI and use that as the basis for your launch template. This could be a server that you manually configure or use a script to provision. Once it's just the way you want it, you can create an Amazon machine image out of that and assign it as the basis for a launch template. Another option is to use user data, which lets you script what you want to happen on startup. Later in this course, we'll show how deployment services like CodeDeploy can issue commands to get new ASG instances in the right state to serve your app. For now, let's see a simple user data example. User data lets you enter shell commands, that'll run in sequence when a new host is added to your ASG. These commands run as root. In this example, we install the web server nginx and start it up, ensuring that any newly provisioned host will run nginx and display its default webpage. In a more complex example, you might also pull down your app from S3 or [GitHub](../../Skills/Software%20Development/GitHub.md), configure it and start it running. Now that we have a launch configuration, we can define an auto scaling group.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understanding-aws-auto-scaling-groups?u=76281980&t=287)** The ASG itself is the simplest part of this process. It has a name, a configuration on where to build new instances, the VPC and subnet, and a starting group size. Scaling policies define the conditions under which your auto scaling group will add or remove capacity in the form of EC2 instances. They're based on CloudWatch alarms. For example, you might say, when CPU usage exceeds 80% for 300 seconds, add one new instance. Cool down settings on the alarm make sure the policy doesn't overreact to a condition and scale up too quickly. For example, you might exceed that 80% CPU threshold for a full five minutes while the scaling policy adds a new instance, but you don't need the alarm to trigger more than once. Other configuration options that you can add to an auto scaling group are defining a load balancer to associate with the group, tagging on the instances, so you can differentiate it from other groups that you create later on, event notifications, so you can receive emails or other alerts about things that happened to your auto-scaling group, and finally scheduled scaling events. By scaling on a schedule, you can plan ahead, if you know that you're going to have increased load at a particular time or on a particular day, you can schedule instances to be added to your ASG automatically before that event occurs.

>

#### [Understanding AWS security groups](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understanding-aws-security-groups?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understanding-aws-security-groups?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] Let's take a look at how [Amazon Web Services](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Amazon%20Web%20Services%20(AWS).md) handles the important topic of [Network Security](../../Skills/Cybersecurity/Network%20Security.md), using [AWS Security](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/AWS%20Security.md) groups. First, a look at a traditional firewall setup. Imagine this is our data center. We have a few [Web Servers](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Web%20Servers.md), maybe a public API host. We also have some private hosts, things like [Databases](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Databases.md) that we want locked down and secure. In our simplified data center, we've divided our space into subnets, public on the left, private on the right, and because we're good system architects concerned about security, we've got a firewall around the whole thing. The firewall protects the whole data center at the border. We define the rules for what traffic goes in and out. We let the good traffic in like our web users, which we allow in on ports 80 and 443. We lock down everything else, keeping the bad guys out. We also configure the firewall to let servers talk out to the world when appropriate, like when we need to call an external API. Of course, the firewall doesn't just protect the border. It can also govern traffic inside the data center, say, between these subnets. These app servers need to talk to databases in the private subnet, so we write rules for that. If you're using an enterprise firewall writing point to point rules, this simple architecture could total up to 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, 7, 8 different rules. Over time as the number of services
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understanding-aws-security-groups?u=76281980&t=95)** and servers increases, this list of rules can become very long and hard to maintain. Even if we define broader rules such as the public subnet is open to all world HTTP traffic, you still have a maintenance issue because troubleshooting connectivity for a particular server means hunting down or knowing all the rules to find somewhere else that may affect it. In fact, sometimes it means you have to sit down and monitor the network traffic first before you even know where to look for the relevant rules. AWS security groups are reusable groups of network security rules. They define incoming and outgoing rules. They can be attached to almost any resource in AWS, such as [EC2](../../Skills/DevOps/Amazon%20EC2.md) instances, load balancers, and IDS databases, and they act like local firewalls for that resource. AWS security groups deny all incoming traffic by default, and they allow all outgoing traffic by default. Any deny is treated as primary to overtake the other rules that may be more specific below it. Let's define a simple security group, for example. This one's intended for EC2 instances hosting web applications. We'll allow HTTPS traffic from the world and SSH traffic from inside private IPs. This will allow us to expose a secure website to web users and lets anyone on our private network log into the machine via SSH. Of course, SSH users will still need a valid login to the machine.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understanding-aws-security-groups?u=76281980&t=188)** We'll keep outgoing rules at their default, fully open, so the machine can call out to [Version Control](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Version%20Control.md) repos, third party APIs, and the like. So here's our data center again. The security group we just defined should be attached to each instance in the public facing web server subnet like so. Because it allows both incoming and outgoing web traffic, it covers fully 1, 2, 3, 4, 5 of the rules we had before. If we define another security group that lets traffic flow from the web server subnet, we can attach it to these database instances and get rid of the last three rules. Finally, there's no need for our expensive firewall appliance. The security groups act like local firewalls for each instance. What about this instance here? Is it vulnerable since there's no longer a border firewall? Not at all. Without a security group, AWS allows no traffic in or out. Advantages of security groups include reuse. You can reuse common sets of network rules. You can easily see where rule sets are applied. You keep network access rules close to the resource they control, and you can apply multiple groups to a single resource. Finally, my favorite feature of AWS security groups. They can be configured not only to allow traffic from certain IP ranges, they can also be configured to allow traffic from certain other security groups. Take the database example we described earlier.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understanding-aws-security-groups?u=76281980&t=284)** It could be configured to allow, say, Postgre's traffic on port 5, 4, 3, 2 from any host in the web subnet range. On the other hand, look at our diagram. Every host the database needs to trust is using the web security group represented in orange. If the blue group could just trust all the oranges, we'd be in business. That way when we create a new web server and attach the web server security group to it, the orange one, we get network connectivity to our databases enabled for free, and we can do just that. Allow traffic from another security group rather than an IP range. See this example here where we provide the security group's ID as a target rather than an IP address. This capability unlocks a whole new way of thinking about network security. In AWS, you can focus less on point-to-point rules in IP ranges and more on what kinds of resources should trust each other. Once you start doing this, a security group starts to look less like a mini firewall and more like a badge that grants you entry to secure places. Features like this really help AWS developers tackle traditional concepts like network security in exciting new ways.

#### [Understanding SSH key authentication](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understanding-ssh-key-authentication?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understanding-ssh-key-authentication?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] When working with [EC2](../../Skills/DevOps/Amazon%20EC2.md) instances, it helps to understand how to connect with SSH key authentication. For the longest time, your only option for logging into an EC2 instance was via SSH. In the next chapter, I'll show you a new and possibly more convenient option using AWS Systems Manager. But for now, let's delve into this useful topic. What's typically required when you want to log into a remote [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) server, like a new Amazon and Linux EC2 instance? Port 22 must be accessible from your location. The host must have SSHD processes running. You must have a username and password and password authentication must be enabled. The client logs in with some SSH client software, such as the Mac's built-in SSH command or PuTTY for [Windows](../../Glossary/Service/Windows.md). The default situation in AWS is a little different. First, AWS instances automatically provision an admin level user for you. On Amazon Linux, this user is called EC2 User. Run two instances, just use root. Check the AWS documentation for your OS type to be sure. Second, they do not provide password login by default. Instead, we'll rely on SSH Key authentication. SSH Key authentication replaces password authentication using public private key encryption. Logging in with your private key requires the remote host has the public key installed. First, you must generate a public private key pair.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understanding-ssh-key-authentication?u=76281980&t=95)** This can be done on Linux or Mac Os with the SSH Key Gen Command. Then you must copy the contents of the public file into the authorized keys file of the remote user. Of course, this requires you to have access to the machine in the first place. Good news, these steps are the ones AWS does for you. AWS will generate a private and public key pair for you. It'll remember the keys it creates and prompt you to use them anytime in the future you need to specify an SSH key. AWS keeps the public key to provision onto instances, but it does not keep the private key. Instead, you'll be given a one-time opportunity to download the private key. You can save it anywhere, but the default location on Mac and Linux systems is home/username/.ssh. The key files are just text files, but you must treat them specially before you can use them. SSH requires that your private key be set to read write only by the file owner with no access to groups or to the world. In Linux file permission terms. This means permission bits zero 400. Treat this file like a password and do not share it. Later, when you create an EC2 instance with this key associated, AWS will automatically provision the public key into the EC2 user's authorized keys file, allowing you to log in as EC2 user by using the corresponding private key. So how do you actually use this key?
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understanding-ssh-key-authentication?u=76281980&t=192)** From a Linux or Mac system it works like this. SSH EC2 user at the remote IP of the host and then the dash I flag giving the path to the private key. This works on most Linux and Mac systems. Don't lose the key or you'll lose access to your instance. AWS does not have any backdoor access to your EC2 instances. They can't help you if you can't get in. One last thing, over time, you may accumulate a number of SSH keys for different services. It can be hard to keep track of what's what. You can make future logins convenient by adding your private key to the SSH key ring. The key ring is a list of private keys that the SSH command will attempt to use when you try to log into a remote host, on Linux or MAC Systems, it's controlled by a command called SSH dash add. Despite its name, you'll use SSH add to add, remove, and list keys in the key ring as shown here. Finally, a warning about the SSH key ring. Some operating systems block your login after five failed attempts in a session. If you have more than five keys on your key ring, you may quickly hit this limit before getting to the right key. If that happens, don't worry. Just use the Capital D flag to remove all your keys, then add back in only the ones that you need. Removing keys from your key ring doesn't delete the key. It just means that it won't be automatically
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understanding-ssh-key-authentication?u=76281980&t=286)** tried until you add it again. As I said, I'll be introducing a simpler way of getting terminal access in the next chapter. However, SSH key authentication is still widely used in many contexts, such as [Server Configuration](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Server%20Configuration.md) management. So it's good to have this knowledge in your toolkit.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

#### [Connect to an EC2 instance with AWS Systems Manager](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/connect-to-an-ec2-instance-with-aws-systems-manager?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/connect-to-an-ec2-instance-with-aws-systems-manager?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] So, we've been able to connect to our instance via SSH, but it's not the most secure thing. Having port 22 open to the world means that any attacker can try to SSH to our machine and gain access. Now of course, they won't have a key to get in, but it would just be more secure if we didn't have that port open at all. In order to connect to this instance without the use of SSH, we're going to use a service called AWS Systems Manager. Now, Systems Manager is a whole service unto itself, which you can reach through the selection here or by searching Systems Manager. And if you look around, you'll see it has all kinds of features for managing fleets of systems. You can use it to install packages on instances, do other kinds of en masse configuration, and we're going to use this later actually when we need to install the CodeDeploy agent on some instances. But for right now, to let Systems Manager help us access our machine, all we need to do is go back to the home console and look for IAM. We'll search that in the search bar. #### [Understanding AWS CloudFormation](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understand-aws-cloudformation?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understand-aws-cloudformation?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] One of the most exciting aspects of AWS is the many ways in which it allows you to build and manage your data center via code. Let's take a look at AWS cloud formation, which does just that. Imagine all the decisions you make when you create resources through the AWS console. Whether you're creating a VPC and subnets, [EC2](../../Skills/DevOps/Amazon%20EC2.md) instances, S3 buckets, or load balancers, you're taking steps to define the configuration setup of each in great detail. The next time you need to create similar resources, you'll be back to clicking through screens, making those same decisions again. It would be great if we could just keep a record and replay those actions. That's where AWS cloud formation comes in because it lets you capture all those decisions and encode them as a [JSON](../../Skills/Web%20Development/JSON.md) or YAML template. Every AWS resource that you can create through the web console has a corresponding YAML format documented by AWS. For instance, if you want to create a VPC structure, a load balancer, and three hosts to register on it, you can do it all through YAML. That YAML representation of related AWS resources is called a stack. A stack file can define just about anything. For instance, it might define some EC2 instances and elastic load balancer, an association between them, and the security groups attached to each. When you run that YAML through cloud formation, it will kick off the creation of all those resources. You can even include parameters in your stack YAML so that users who execute the file can change the outcome by entering different values.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understand-aws-cloudformation?u=76281980&t=95)** For instance, using the same stack instead of hard coding a desired size for an auto-scaling group, we could parameterize it. Now that YAML can be used to create similar stacks with varied configurations, we could parameterize all kinds of things like network location, security group ports, EC2 instance type. Really anything. In this way, a single template can be used to build many stacks of similar nature. Another great feature of cloud formation is that it cleans up after itself. If you delete an instance of a stack cloud formation terminates it and all the resources it created. There are some exceptions to this behavior, but for the most part, cloud formation lets you manage a stack as a single entity. Of course, one of the best features of cloud formation is that if you use it from the beginning, your entire data center infrastructure, VPCs, subnets, load balancers, EC2 instances, RDS [Databases](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Databases.md), and more, is now defining code. Code can be version controlled, code can be shared, modified, reviewed, say in a [GitHub](../../Skills/Software%20Development/GitHub.md) pull request, and reused. Some additional cloud formation features you might want to know about. You can create conditional logic. For example, you might change a security group based on the value of a parameter. You can push updates to a stack instead of just creating or deleting. You can call custom functions to extend existing functionality, and of course, you get to maintain your [infrastructure as code](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Infrastructure%20as%20code%20(IaC).md).
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understand-aws-cloudformation?u=76281980&t=188)** Another useful feature of cloud formation is cloud formation designer, which lets you create templates in an easy to use drag and drop format. While you connect resource icons, cloud formation designer is building the corresponding JSON or YAML template on the backend. At any time, you can edit the template directly, then save the result to your local file system or to S3. You can even create stacks directly from here. This interface can help you understand the many parameters required to build resources in cloud formation, and as a bonus, the finished product tends to resemble the kind of [System Architecture](../../Skills/Software%20Development/System%20Architecture.md) diagrams you might have otherwise created as a separate step. For those of you using VS code as your editor, I highly recommend these two extensions: cloud formation linter and cloud formation snippets. They can save a ton of time, not only scaffolding out your template files, but can help you understand parameter values with intelligent auto complete and help catch mistakes early by highlighting invalid syntax or inconsistent references. In this course, we'll create a template from scratch, but for anyone looking to level up their cloud formation game with tips like these and more, please see my other course, advanced cloud formation for enterprise in this site's library. Now, let's go hands on with our first template.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Ec2](../../Skills/DevOps/Amazon%20EC2.md) (4), [JSON](../../Skills/Web%20Development/JSON.md) (2), [Databases](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Databases.md) (1), [GitHub](../../Skills/Software%20Development/GitHub.md) (1), [Infrastructure as code](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Infrastructure%20as%20code%20(IaC).md) (1)
> **Env Vars:** yaml (8), aws (7), ec2 (4), vpc (2), json (2)
> **CLI Commands:** aws (7), make (1)
> **Exercise Files:** template (7)
> **Analogies:** for instance (3), imagine (1), for example (1)
> **Tools:** aws console (1), github (1), vs code (1)
> **Prerequisites:** setup (1), required to (1)
> **UI Navigation:** drag and drop (1)

#### [Writing an AWS CloudFormation template](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/writing-an-aws-cloudformation-template?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/writing-an-aws-cloudformation-template?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] All right, let's create a few resources using CloudFormation. If you're new to CloudFormation, this document here is a great place to get started. Here in the AWS Documentation Center, I've searched for something called template anatomy. This doc shows an outline of all the sections that you need to create a CloudFormation template. You can see there's a [JSON](../../Skills/Web%20Development/JSON.md) section here, and a YAML section down below. Either one is valid, but we'll use YAML for readability. You can see that we need the three hyphens that make a YAML file at the top. We'll need AWSTemplateFormatVersion, that's required. And the [rest](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Representational%20State%20Transfer%20(REST).md) are optional except for Resources, because of course we'll need to define some resources for this template to create. Beyond resources, in this example, we'll use both Description and Parameters. So let's go to a text editor and start to lay out the format of what this template is going to look like. I'll copy that. So we'll start with the three hyphens, and this value, AWSTemplateFormatVersion. I've set my editor to YAML syntax, so we should get some nice highlighting. The value I'm going to get here is one that you can use as well, '2010-09-09'. That's a valid value, and other than that, don't really worry about it. It's not going to change the behavior of your CloudFormation template.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/writing-an-aws-cloudformation-template?u=76281980&t=94)** Now let's go ahead and just lay out the top level sections. We're going to have Description here, we're going to have Parameters, and we're going to have Resources, and we'll expand on each one in turn. Description, we'll call Single-instance web server. That's what we're going to build. We'll skip Parameters for now and go on to Resources. Hit Enter, and give two spaces to indent. This is YAML, so of course, your formatting is really important to this getting parsed correctly. Two spaces as an indentation. And now we're going to give our resource, our first resource and only resource in this template, a name. This name can be whatever we want. So we're going to call this MyInstance with a colon at the end, hit Enter, and again, indent two spaces. So that's going to be the name tag for this resource that we're about to create. And what type of resource? Well, we're going to have Type. And we're going to enter a special value here. This is AWS::[EC2](../../Skills/DevOps/Amazon%20EC2.md)::Instance. You see, every single resource that you can create in CloudFormation has a special resource type string that you can discover in the AWS docs. There's a documentation section for every resource as to how you build it in CloudFormation. And in fact, we're about to take a look at that for EC2. Before we go over there, let's type Properties:,
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/writing-an-aws-cloudformation-template?u=76281980&t=190)** and indent again. Now we need to know what are the kinds of properties that we can put on an EC2 YAML definition. Let's go back to the documentation. I've opened up another doc here that I searched up, that is the EC2 syntax for CloudFormation. And you can see right here at the top, the reference to the type name. You can also see it in the examples below. I'll scroll down to the YAML section. So there's the type that we need to use, and then all the possible properties of which there are quite a few. Now, if you went through the video where we created an instance through the wizard, you understand that there are a lot of choices that you can make when creating an EC2 instance. We're only going to need a few of these though, like ImageId, SubnetId and SecurityGroups. If you search in this doc for the [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) required, you can see that each property has a value that says whether it's required or not, or whether it's conditional, which means it's required if some other condition is met or some other parameter is used. So let's head back to our text editor and fill in the properties that we are going to use on this instance. We're going to have just a couple of things here. Go to a new line, indent two spaces, and we'll list them off. We're going to have UserData. Remember, UserData is our opportunity to give some shell script to the instance when it starts up. In a moment, we're going to use this section to install nginx and start it running. The next section is going to be ImageId.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/writing-an-aws-cloudformation-template?u=76281980&t=284)** This will be the ID of the AMI that we're going to use as a base for this image, in our case, Amazon and [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) 2. Then we have InstanceType, that'll be the sizing of this instance. SecurityGroupIds will be an array of SecurityGroups.
>
> **[5:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/writing-an-aws-cloudformation-template?u=76281980&t=305)** SubnetId, which we need to place the instance. And finally, IamInstanceProfile, which we'll use to provide the SSM role that will let us use Systems Manager to log into this instance. Okay, let's start filling these out. The only thing I'm going to hard code here, other than UserData, is the ImageId. So to get the idea of an AMI that we'll use to build this instance, let's head over to the EC2 console. I'll click into EC2 here from the AWS homepage, and click Launch instances. I'm not actually going to launch an instance, I just want to see this page here where we get to choose an Amazon machine image. And I can see in the base Amazon Linux 2 AMI, along with its ID here. We've got two options, an x86 and an ARM version. So I'm going to go with the x86, just copy that value, head back to my text editor, one space, and paste it in. I don't need double quotes or anything. YAML will parse it as is. All right, for some of the others, we're going to turn to our Parameters section now. We want the user to be able to fill in values for each of these. Remember the idea with CloudFormation templates is that they are just that, they're templates. You can define something that can be used in many different ways based on the values that people put in when they execute it. So the same template could be used to create many different stacks that have different attributes and behaviors. So we're going to put a new line here,
>
> **[6:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/writing-an-aws-cloudformation-template?u=76281980&t=398)** and again, indent two spaces, and we're going to create some names for some parameters. So like the names on resources, these parameter names are arbitrary. They're made up by us and they can be anything, but we're going to give them descriptive names, because these names will appear to users in the CloudFormation console when they're asked to provide values. So the values we're going to ask them to provide are the Subnet, the IamInstanceProfile, the SecurityGroup, not SecurityGroupId, I'll just say SecurityGroup, and the InstanceType. As long as these names are referenced exactly, later when we refer to them back in the template, then everything will work. We don't have to worry about name conflicts, like IamInstanceProfile, which is a key down here for a property and is also the name of one of our parameters. We'll make it clear, which we're referring to by the keywords we use. Each one of these can take a description, which could be useful, to provide more context for the user who's putting these inputs in. So we can say Subnet, Description, where the instance will be placed, and we can give descriptions for everything. Maybe even a hint, like here under IamInstanceProfile, we could say, it would be a good idea to put the name of the SSM instance rule here. SecurityGroup and InstanceType,
>
> **[8:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/writing-an-aws-cloudformation-template?u=76281980&t=490)** they don't really need descriptions, so we'll leave 'em off. But every parameter does need something called Type, just like resources. So I'll start with InstanceType and kind of work my way up. InstanceType's type will be string, and I can give it a default value. How about t3.micro? Now that's great. And if I leave it just like this, of type string, the user will see the text input with the default value of t3.micro, which they are free to backspace over and type something else in. And as long as it's a valid instance type, it'll work. But we can make things a little bit easier for them, or possibly constrain values when we want to do that, by using something called AllowedValues. So say AllowedValues:, and we'll start a YAML array here by using hyphens, and we'll give some options, t3.nano. Another hyphen, t3.micro, let's go ahead and give the one that is default. t3.small, you know, we can put a different in, m1.medium, something like that. So if we do that, these are the only four values that will be allowed, and that'll come across as a dropdown. In the SecurityGroup we'll give a type, a special type defined by AWS called AWS::EC2::SecurityGroup::Id.
>
> **[9:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/writing-an-aws-cloudformation-template?u=76281980&t=583)** When we give it this special parameter type here, this will cause AWS to do a little bit of user interface magic for us and provide a dropdown that has all the SecurityGroups in our account. Really helpful. We can do the same thing for InstanceProfile. We'll give that a type. There's no special type for this, unfortunately. We just need to make it a string. And what's even more important to note is that, unlike some places where you're putting a string value in, this is not asking for an ID, doesn't want an ARN or any other identifier. It just wants the name of a role. So I've put that here in the description, that you're going to put the name of the role. Subnet though does have a special type, which is AWS::EC2::Subnet::Id. Now we've got this parameter section. It's all going to show up as a question for the user that inputs this template, but we need to refer to these values down in the template itself. So we'll start with InstanceType. Let me show how that works. I'll create a new line, two spaces, the word Ref, R-E-F, colon, and then a reference back to the parameter name, which is InstanceType. So this must match this, and as long as it does, it's going to work. We could give these different names. We could say MyInstanceType and that might make things a little more comprehensible, because you could get confused between the name of the parameter and the name of the key that we're defining.
>
> **[11:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/writing-an-aws-cloudformation-template?u=76281980&t=677)** But as long as you keep things straight and make sure the parameters match the refs, it's all going to work out. SecurityGroupId, I've made a small mistake here. This is SecurityGroupIds with an S. So make sure you have that, indent, because what this is looking for is a YAML array, because you could put many SecurityGroups on an instance. What we're going to do is have an array of just one value, because we're only asking the user in Parameters to give us just one value. SecurityGroupIds, we'll say Ref again, and type SecurityGroup. This refers back to the parameter name. SubnetId is going to work the same way as InstanceType. It's a single value, so we'll say Ref, and then Subnet. IamInstanceProfile, again, same thing, single value, Ref, and then IamInstanceProfile. At this point, we're almost done, but we still do want to provide user data, because this is where we'll put the shell script that installs nginx. We use a function called Fn::Base64. So Fn::Base64, that's going to encode whatever comes next as Base64 for use in UserData. Another function !Sub, will let us define script over multiple lines, follow it with this pipe character, and then we can indent and basically type freely. We'll go #!/bin/bash -xe. That's going to invoke Bash. So the rest of the script gets executed as shell script.
>
> **[12:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/writing-an-aws-cloudformation-template?u=76281980&t=776)** We'll say sudo amazon-linux-extras install nginx1 -y.
>
> **[13:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/writing-an-aws-cloudformation-template?u=76281980&t=785)** So what's going on in this command? Well, this is a Linux system. It's based on CentOS. So you might think, you could just say sudo yum install nginx, but it turns out that package is not available. Instead, Amazon provides this command here, amazon-linux-extras, which is its own package manager for a few extra things that you might want on an Amazon Linux system, including Nginx, which has the package name nginx1, And the -y will make sure that the instance does not wait for interactive user input to say yes or no as to whether it should install. It will just go ahead. Then we say sudo service nginx start. Notice that the package name is nginx1, but you still have the executable service name of just nginx. So with this, our template is done and we can save it. So now we're done with this template except for an issue that I've just noticed here, a typo that I made earlier in the Type value for the instance, it should be AWS::EC2::Instance, not MyInstance. So make sure it's expressed just as I've got it here, AWS::EC2::Instance, and save the file as I've already done. You can give it a name like single-instance.yaml like me, and have it somewhere handy where we can upload a CloudFormation in the next step.

>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/running-an-aws-cloudformation-template?u=76281980&t=188)** as a stack, that's why it's got that name, it's a stack. #### [Concepts for updating an AWS CloudFormation stack](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/concepts-for-updating-an-aws-cloudformation-stack?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/concepts-for-updating-an-aws-cloudformation-stack?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Once a stack has been created, CloudFormation allows you to update the resources managed by the stack. Updating a stack allows you to modify parameter inputs or change the template itself. The resulting action will only update the resources you modify. Be advised that this action could disrupt running services. Finally, updating a stack comes in two flavors, direct update or change sets. Direct updates occur immediately. Change sets on the other hand, introduce some additional steps to make sure everything is going to end up the way you want. Change sets let you preview changes. When updating a CloudFormation stack. They clarify how CloudFormation will execute a change. For instance, will it update a resource or delete and recreate it? However, they do not and cannot indicate the successful outcome of a change. Change sets can be executed right away or saved for later, and they expire once the underlying template is updated. After all, a set of proposed changes may not make sense once the stack has been updated. Let's head into CloudFormation and see how it works.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **CLI Commands:** make (2)
> **Exercise Files:** template (2)
> **Analogies:** for instance (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

>
> **[6:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/practice-updating-aws-cloudformation-stacks-via-change-sets?u=76281980&t=379)** that takes place against my instance, which is a reference back to the name in our template of that instance. Here's the type that it is, here's the ID that it currently has. And very important here, replacement. In this case, replacement is true because if we take this action, we know we can't just reboot an instance to change its operating system, we have to completely tear it down, terminate it, and build it back up again. So we've got to change set here available, but maybe this isn't exactly what we want to do. Instead, let's head back using the breadcrumb trail and go to InstanceAndSecurityGroup, our stack. And click next. Again, scroll through here, scroll through the summary, click Create change set. And this one we'll call VerticalScaling 'cause we're just going to up that instance type to something bigger. Click Create change set, and then refresh. Now that the change set is ready, you can see something slightly different. We still have a modify action on that same instance, but now, replacement doesn't say true. It says conditional. And you might think, well,
>
> **[7:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/practice-updating-aws-cloudformation-stacks-via-change-sets?u=76281980&t=471)** all we're doing is changing the instance type, we know that that's not going to actually tear down the instance and build it back, but it says conditional because the answer to whether this instance gets replaced actually depends on how you built the instance in the first place. There are a couple of options. The primary one of which is using instance back storage, which is a rare thing that you probably don't want to do, but if you did it, AWS supports it. And that kind of instance can't be just rebooted into a new instance size. So because AWS continues to support that kind of instance building, the answer here is conditional. But of course we know from experience that we can change an instance type with just a simple reboot. So we know that that's going to be a false for a replace. All right, let's head back to the stacks. Go back to instance and security group. And now we're on a tab called Change sets. You see that we have both change sets available to us. We can choose one, let's do vertical scaling and go Execute. You see, now we know because we've created the change sets, what they're going to do to our stack. We're a little bit more informed and we can click Execute. We have the same options that we have when creating a brand new stack as to how it should handle failures. Roll back all stack resources is fine. So click Execute change set. And now here on the events tab, you'll see we get a few new events as things happen from the change set. Okay, we now have update complete.
>
> **[9:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/practice-updating-aws-cloudformation-stacks-via-change-sets?u=76281980&t=564)** So this change set has been executed Before we go check it out, I want to point out one thing. If you click on Change sets on the tab here, you'll see we no longer have any change sets. We had two, we executed one, and now we have zero. And the reason is CloudFormation can no longer guarantee that the changes described in a change set will still hold now that you've actually changed the stack. So if you want to see what happens with another change or a change you've already put in as a set, you're going to have to do it again to let CloudFormation reevaluate the impact. All right, to see our change, let's head to the other tab here that is still on EC2. I guess I'll hit back here. And you can see now that our AmazonLinuxInstance is indeed running as a t2.micro, so it has gone up in size. So that's a look at how change sets will give you an opportunity to look at the results of your actions, changing a CloudFormation template before you ever have to commit anything.

#### [Understanding AWS CloudFormation custom resources](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understand-aws-cloudformation-custom-resources?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understand-aws-cloudformation-custom-resources?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] CloudFormation is one of AWS's most frequently enhanced services, but it can't do everything. Sometimes you need to manage a resource that CloudFormation doesn't support, but you still want to do it in the context of CloudFormation's [infrastructure as code](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Infrastructure%20as%20code%20(IaC).md). That's where custom resources come in. Custom resources allow you to execute your own functions from with your CloudFormation template, make and accept input parameters and return output parameters. They can be used to create, update or destroy resources, just like any CloudFormation resource or to perform any other task. They are backed by a Lambda function that you will write. Use cases for custom resources include supporting a new AWS resource before AWS itself adds official CloudFormation support, fetching values that you'll want to use later in the template, such as an AMI ID, performing provisioning steps, like seeding a database or basically anything else you can think of to do within a Lambda function. In this demo, we're going to create a custom resource to manage S3. Of course, CloudFormation can already do this, but it's a good example of how things will work. Our final template will work by making an SDK call to the S3 service. This call will be invoked by a Lambda function, also defined in the template. That function is going to need an IAM role that allows it to make the SDK call to S3, so we'll create and use a role that allows full S3 access.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understand-aws-cloudformation-custom-resources?u=76281980&t=95)** Finally, we'll call the Lambda function as a custom resource within the CloudFormation template. Let's head to the console now to try it out.
