### 3. Deployment Concepts

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Architect apps for horizontal scaling](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/architect-apps-for-horizontal-scaling?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/architect-apps-for-horizontal-scaling?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When we talk about building apps for the cloud, it's good to think about scaling from the outset. Planning to scale starts with how you build your apps. When you build an application, you hope that it will attract a wide audience. Of course, the more concurrent users your app attracts, the more you're going to need to prepare to make sure that your app has the capacity to serve them well. Let's assume your application is reasonably well-performing on its own. Your app has the basic traits of a well-performing application. The server requests are as small and fast as possible. Network calls and file I/O are minimized, or avoided when possible. And resource-intensive processes are run asynchronously in the background, not occupying web workers. You've done all that and more, and you're just running up against the limits of your application server. Maybe you're close to maxing out CPU or RAM. It's time to scale. Scaling a web app comes in two flavors, vertical and horizontal. Vertical scaling means to simply increase the resources on a single server. This means adding [Hardware](../../Topics/Hardware.md), more RAM, faster CPU, that sort of thing. Or in AWS terms, changing to a more powerful instance type. Whether it's an AWS instance, or physical hardware, scaling vertically means incurring some downtime on that machine as you upgrade. Even an [EC2](../../Skills/DevOps/Amazon%20EC2.md) instance has to at least reboot when changing type. Horizontal scaling refers to distributing your application over multiple servers.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/architect-apps-for-horizontal-scaling?u=76281980&t=94)** Each server is alike, running a copy of your application. A device called a load balancer, such as AWS's Application Load Balancer, sits in front of the group and coordinates traffic among them. Horizontal scaling has a number of advantages over vertical scaling. First, better cost scaling. It can be cheaper to achieve the same capacity. It gets expensive to build and run highly provisioned machines, price tends to scale up faster when you scale vertically. It can also provide more fault tolerance. If a single host web app has a hardware problem, you're toast. In a multi-host environment, hardware failures may mean you lose capacity for a while, but they don't necessarily mean you're offline. Finally, it requires no downtime to scale. When scaling horizontally, increasing or decreasing capacity is much easier, especially if you use services like AWS Auto-Scaling Groups, which allow you to define scaling criteria that add or remove EC2 instances when you need them. So, horizontal scaling is great, right? Yes, but your app needs to be built with it in mind. First, you must make sure to separate the tiers of your application. You might be used to installing a database, like [MySQL](../../Skills/Software%20Development/MySQL.md), on the same host as your web app. This image represents a single web server configured in just that way. The app server talks to the database, like so. But when you're horizontally scaling, the idea is to distribute load over multiple copies of your server, and load balance with something like an ALB. So, what then?
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/architect-apps-for-horizontal-scaling?u=76281980&t=187)** Do you build a new host with its own database? No, the [Databases](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Databases.md) will immediately be out of sync, and your app will be broken. You could point New Host to the first server's database, but that's a disaster waiting to happen. What happens if the first web server experiences hardware failure? Your database goes with it. Instead, you need to separate your concerns. Dedicate the app server to being just an app server. Build the database separately, then point the app server there. Then new app servers can be built to replicate the first, and point to the same database. Of course, you'll need a load balancer at this point. This multi-tier model allows you to scale each layer independently. Load balancers and database engines tend to scale better than application servers, so you might end up with a diamond-shaped architecture, like this. Also critical to supporting horizontal scaling, avoiding the file system. Remember this picture? The problem was that a single critical resource needed by every application server, in this case, the database, was locked to a single instance. In fact, you can get into trouble with any use of the local file system, which creates a dependency on a single node. For instance, if you're storing user session information on the file system, your users will have problems when you scale to multiple hosts. Imagine you have your nice end-tier architecture, but you're still storing session information on the file system.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/architect-apps-for-horizontal-scaling?u=76281980&t=278)** If this user's shopping cart info is stored on Server A, what happens when the load balancer sends their next click to Server B? The cart is gone. The same problem can occur if you allow users to upload data, such as profile pictures to your app, if they get stored on a local file system, users routed to other hosts will not see them. Some load balancers support the concept of sticky sessions, meaning that the load balancer is smart enough to always send this particular user to Node A, but that's only for the current session, and not something we want to rely on. Instead, look at alternate solutions. Chances are that your application framework allows you to specify where session information is stored. Ruby on Rails, for instance, lets you specify active record as its store. If you point active record to a remote database, you're good. That's point one, use database-backed or cookie session stores. If you need to let users upload assets, consider storing those in the database, or even better, look at cloud-based storage like S3. That's point two. Finally, if your application absolutely requires a local volume to function, take a look at AWS's Elastic File System, or EFS. Unlike Elastic Block Store, EFS can scale itself, and be mounted by multiple instances, meaning your application servers can share the volume, and horizontal scaling still works. For more on EFS, see my other course, AWS Storage and [Data Management](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Data%20Management.md).

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>

#### [AWS network security for app deployment](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-network-security-for-app-deployment?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-network-security-for-app-deployment?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In this series, you'll occasionally see me demo a service in a way that I explain is not suitable for production. For example, if the point of a video is to show how to build an [EC2](../../Skills/DevOps/Amazon%20EC2.md) instance, I might have you configure its security groups to allow SSH from anywhere in the world. That's great for a simple demo, but it's not the most secure way to implement this access. Let's take a moment to dig into some [Network Security](../../Skills/Cybersecurity/Network%20Security.md) best practices for building your apps on AWS. That way when we take shortcuts for demo purposes, you'll have this architecture in mind for the real thing. Let's say you have a web application, you want to be world accessible, and it has a database in RDS. First, let's talk about your network architecture. You're going to want a private subnet and a public subnet. A private subnet is one that has no internet gateway, so by default it cannot reach out to the internet, and the internet can't reach in. To learn in detail how to set up and configure VPCs and subnets in this way. Search for the course titled [Amazon Web Services](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Amazon%20Web%20Services%20(AWS).md) Networking in our library of courses. Now, for example, with an instance here with a public IP and a security group that allows TCP 80 from the world, you could host a static webpage that's publicly accessible. You could, but you shouldn't. For one thing, HTTP traffic on port 80 is not encrypted. That's not safe for users who might enter values like passwords or even just data they don't want to others see.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-network-security-for-app-deployment?u=76281980&t=95)** Plus, if you set this up, you probably went ahead and made port 22 public so that you could SSH to this machine and configure and maintain it. That's much worse. Why? Because now the only thing between a hacker and the inside of your VPC is the strength of your password or the security of your SSH key. Once a single host gets compromised, the bad guys are inside and everything else in the VPC is at risk. And since you may not be patching your systems quite as often as you should, they could be vulnerable to all kinds of attacks you're not aware of. So let's not do that. Instead, let's use an ALB. It's going to be the one thing in this diagram configured for world access. Set it up as an internet facing type, and create listeners and assign security groups that allow port 443, not 80 to the world. Or if you like, open both. ALBs have an option to redirect HTTP to HTTPS, which is a common and welcome practice for most websites today. When you create the Port 443 HTTPS listener on the ALB, you're going to be required to upload a certificate. This will be a TLS formerly SSL certificate that allows the ALB to serve encrypted HTTPS traffic. Although it's outside the scope of this series, I would highly recommend using AWS's certificate manager service. It will automatically generate certs for any domain that you own. All you have to do is verify your ownership of that domain
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-network-security-for-app-deployment?u=76281980&t=189)** by inputting a special value in its DNS record. Then AWS manages the cert and it can be set to automatically renew it. Another great feature of cert manager is that it's centralized. If you apply the same cert to multiple load bouncers, they can all be rotated or updated at once. Now you can ditch the public IP of this instance, create a security group for the instance that allows port 80 traffic from the ALB's security group. Remember, a security group rule can use another security group as a source. Create a target group on the ALB that points to your app's instance or maybe auto-scaling group if you're already thinking about horizontally scaling and why not? You have a load balancer already and an auto-scaling group size of one costs you no more than a single instance by itself. Then you have the group ready to scale if you need it. But I digress. At this point, traffic can reach your host securely and no public traffic is reaching your instance except through the ALB. Now, don't forget, we're actually trying to model a web application with a database tier. So let's get that RDS instance back in here. It's going to live in the private subnet, no internet in or out, and we'll create a security group that allows the web tier to come in on its database port. It's even possible given this architecture to move the app tier into the private subnet, but of course, we still will have to account for managing the EC2 instance doing that critical patching, for example. So let's just shift that dividing line over a little bit.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-network-security-for-app-deployment?u=76281980&t=285)** Now you're going to run into a little difficulty. For instance, when you do want to patch, your instance needs to download package updates. Or when your app deploys and needs to do a [Git](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Git.md) pull, it will need to get to the internet and there's no gateway. Well, that's where a NAT gateway comes in. This is an AWS service that allows traffic out of a VPC. But because there's no internet gateway, the opposite is still not true, meaning that this is still a private subnet. But still, I'm sure you're wondering about our admin user in their laptop. How do they log into the host? Well, if that's a necessity for you, and maybe you're using [Configuration Management](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Configuration%20Management.md) or Docker so you're not manually logging into host, but if you need to do it, you have two options. The first and best option is to use something like AWS Direct Connect to establish a VPN tunnel between your VPC and a local data center. You can find a great video on this subject by searching AWS Direct Connect in the video library on this site. With a connection like this, you can be inside your on-premises data center physically or virtually by use of a VPN, then access your instance on port 22 via its private IP. That's the ideal method, but it requires a bit of setup and perhaps more infrastructure than you have, including [Hardware](../../Topics/Hardware.md) on your local side. If you can't go to the effort of creating a truly private tunnel to your AWS account, another method is the so-called jump box or bastion host method.
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-network-security-for-app-deployment?u=76281980&t=378)** In this case, you can't avoid having public access, but you can limit your exposure by having just one host that allows SSH from the world. This is the jump box, and because it's exposed, you must be very careful to harden the operating system and all software on it to ensure security. This is where it makes a lot of sense to work with experienced information security and OS system engineers. So remember, don't make resources world accessible by default. Don't expose unnecessary ports. Don't skip TLS, but do adhere to the principle of least privilege. Adopt a client-server security group model. Keep data in private subnets. patch your systems frequently and regularly. And use ALBs to expose world accessible resources with TLS configured.

### 4. Elastic Beanstalk

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Understanding AWS Elastic Beanstalk](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understand-aws-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understand-aws-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Elastic Beanstalk is AWS's application [platform as a service](../../Glossary/Concept/PaaS.md). Before we delve into platform as a service though, it might help to take one step back and understand [infrastructure as a service](../../Glossary/Concept/IaaS.md). Infrastructure as a service platforms, like AWS, give you the tools to easily create and manage many components of a data center that would formally have involved manual effort and physical [Hardware](../../Topics/Hardware.md). AWS has several great examples of infrastructure as a service. Think of all the details that AWS takes care of when you build a VPC, subnets, [EC2](../../Skills/DevOps/Amazon%20EC2.md) instances, load balancers, or security groups. You don't need to know how to install, deploy, and scale a load balancer. AWS abstracts that complexity and frees you up to spend time on higher level problems, such as how you want to architect your systems. Platform as a service takes things a step further. As a web developer, you may be familiar with the many steps it takes to get an application server up and running. But with platform as a service, those details are handled for you. All you need to care about is developing your app and pushing it to the platform. Elastic Beanstalk offers platform as a service for web applications using many popular languages, letting you focus on your application. It gives you all the tools to deploy, run, and scale your app with minimal intervention, while still giving you the flexibility to configure the environment as needed. AWS Elastic Beanstalk provides this capability for many popular web programming languages, such as Ruby on Rails, [Python](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Python%20(Programming%20Language).md), [Node.js](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Node.js.md),
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understand-aws-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=94)** [PHP](../../Skills/Software%20Development/PHP.md), Go, [Java](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Java.md), and .NET. That's not all. Let's [Zoom](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Zoom.md) in on the app server because we need to do some provisioning. Often a web server runs both an application server and a web server, which proxies request to the app server. My preferred Ruby on Rails app stack starts with NGINX, which serves static content and proxies all other requests to a Rails app server. And to actually run the app, I'll need an up-to-date version of Ruby, along with Bundler, the RubyGem package manager. Good news, Beanstalk will take care of all of this for us: load balancing, web server provisioning, [Databases](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Databases.md), and more. So let's look at the components of an Elastic Beanstalk application, and the first step is to define an application. An application has a name and an associated stack. In Beanstalk terms, a stack is the choice of language version and application server you wish to use. After that, we define environments. Environments are just what you might expect. Definitions of environments such as staging for production. Later, we'll deploy application revisions to these environments using the AWS console or the Elastic Beanstalk command line tools. And even as Beanstalk enables us to forget about the details of both infrastructure and platform, it still gives us flexibility in how you run the application. For instance, you can choose among several combinations of Ruby version application server and operating system. These combinations are called stacks, and you'll find a choice of stack
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understand-aws-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=188)** for every web application platform that Beanstalk supports. Environment creation is where Beanstalk creates everything you need to get started deploying. First, it creates an Auto Scaling group indicated by the black dashed line here. That Auto Scaling group is associated with a load balancer shown here. The load balancer can be paired with an SSL certificate to serve HTTPS traffic. The Auto Scaling group starts with a default size of one, so you get an EC2 instance here. That instance will be automatically provisioned with the software required by your selected platform and stack. In the Ruby example, this host is an Amazon [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) instance with Ruby and NGINX installed. That host also has an agent running shown here. This agent enables detailed monitoring of your environment. You can optionally ask Beanstalk to create an RDS database for your apps shown here. Each component, the ASG, load balancer, and RDS database, is protected by a security group. The rules differ by component, but for example, the load balancer allows port 80 traffic by default. Finally, AWS uses Route 53 to point a DNS name to the load balancer. This is the same kind of long, unwieldy DNS name that AWS provisions to EC2 instances. Just like that, Beanstalk takes a ton of work off our hands. We instantly have the infrastructure we need plus server instances provision with a required software already installed and configured. We also get management of load balancing in SSL.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understand-aws-elastic-beanstalk?u=76281980&t=283)** Beanstalk can deploy applications stored in S3 or in a [Git](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Git.md) remote, such as [GitHub](../../Skills/Software%20Development/GitHub.md), [Bitbucket](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Bitbucket.md), or AWS's own CodeCommit. The Beanstalk [CLI](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/CLI.md), or command line interface, can perform all environment creation and management tasks and deploy and update applications. Additional features of Beanstalk include enhanced health checks and environmental monitoring. If you'd like to explore more about Beanstalk, you can look into worker tiers, which are applications deployed that do not have a user interface, and container-based deployments, which use Docker containers. Also, if you're a Java or a .NET developer, search the AWS docs for details on plugins for Eclipse or Visual Studio that add integrated Beanstalk deployment tools to the native experience.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Platform as a service](../../Glossary/Concept/PaaS.md) (5), [Infrastructure as a service](../../Glossary/Concept/IaaS.md) (3), [Ec2](../../Skills/DevOps/Amazon%20EC2.md) (3), [Java](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Java.md) (2), [Hardware](../../Topics/Hardware.md) (1)
> **Env Vars:** aws (11), ec2 (3), net (2), nginx (2), ssl (2)
> **CLI Commands:** aws (11), ruby (6), rails (3), nginx (2), python (1)
> **Analogies:** such as (4), for instance (1), for example (1), just like (1)
> **Tools:** command line (2), aws console (1), github (1), bitbucket (1), visual studio (1)
> **Prerequisites:** install (1), configure (1)
> **File Paths:** node.js (1)
> **Ports:** port 80 (1)

#### [AWS Elastic Beanstalk deployment options](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-elastic-beanstalk-deployment-options?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-elastic-beanstalk-deployment-options?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Now that we've created an Elastic Beanstalk app and environment, our infrastructure is waiting for us to deploy a code. Let's take a look at the options Beanstalk makes available to us. We can deploy from the command line with the AWS Beanstalk command line tool, which also gives us commands to build, monitor, and configure EB environments. We can also upload applications via the Elastic Beanstalk web console. No matter which method we choose, each time we deploy a new application version, Beanstalk stores it in S3. So we always have a history of what we've deployed. Beanstalk also gives us flexibility on how we deploy. In a single instance environment, deployments will be instantaneous. Our app will be unavailable to users while the new code is being deployed. In a rolling deployment, individual instances are removed from the load balancer. While they're disconnected and not serving requests to users, Beanstalk deploys the new application revision to them. After the new version of the app comes online, Beanstalk re-adds that instance to the load balancer and moves on. A rolling deployment can happen one instance at a time, or if you have many instances, any number or percentage you specify can be brought down and upgraded at once. Rolling deployments are zero downtime deployments because there's always at least one application host ready to serve users. If your user load is great, your capacity will suffer as instances drop out. Consider increasing the number of instances
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-elastic-beanstalk-deployment-options?u=76281980&t=94)** in your auto-scaling group prior to deployment to avoid a capacity hit. An immutable, or blue/green deployment, goes even further in avoiding downtime for your application. In this model, Beanstalk creates an entirely new auto scaling group, populates it with instances to match your existing group, and deploys the latest application revision to them. When this clone copy of your environment is green, Beanstalk switches the load balancer to this new group and deletes the old one. With these options, Beanstalk allows you maximum flexibility on how your application changes are propagated across your environment, no matter how big or small they may be.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

> **CLI Commands:** aws (1)
> **Env Vars:** aws (1)
> **Prerequisites:** configure (1)

#### [Customize AWS Elastic Beanstalk with ebextensions](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/customize-aws-elastic-beanstalk-with-ebextensions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/customize-aws-elastic-beanstalk-with-ebextensions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Though Elastic Beanstalk provides instances that are pre-provisioned with most of the software you need for your chosen stack, sometimes it is necessary to augment the base configuration with additional setup steps. To help with this, Beanstalk allows users to customize instances provisioned by Beanstalk using a special directory called .ebextensions. This directory must be in the app's root folder and be added to the app revision. Finally, it will contain config files that will be evaluated in alphabetical order. Here's an example of a config file we might place inside .ebextensions. The file's name is basic.config, but it can be called whatever you like as long as the extension is .config. This snippet uses three of .ebextensions configuration sections coded in YAML. First, packages. In this section, we tell Beanstalk to install the package samba-client using yum, the package manager native to Amazon [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md). The packages section also supports package managers [Python](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Python%20(Programming%20Language).md), RPM, and even Ruby Gems. Next, options_settings. This is our opportunity to define environment variables without needing to use the eb set env command line command. Note that we're required to specify the namespace, aws:elasticbeanstalk:application:environment. Be advised also that you should not put sensitive values here. If you need to inject passwords
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/customize-aws-elastic-beanstalk-with-ebextensions?u=76281980&t=92)** or other valuable secrets into the runtime environment, consider either using the Elastic Beanstalk command line to push environment variables and trigger a deploy or modify your app to [Fetch](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Fetch.md) secrets from a secure repository, like AWS's Parameter Store, which we'll explore in the next chapter. Finally, container_commands, which is our chance to execute any command we need to run. Notice how there are two named sections underneath container commands, 01deploy and 02deploy. These labels can be anything you like, but I've named them that way to ensure proper ordering as these commands are executed in alphabetic order. If you place this file in a .ebextensions folder, commit it to your [Git](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Git.md) repo and deploy your Beanstalk environment, all of this will get executed for each instance that joins your environment. A .ebextensions file can contain some other sections that give you control over the environment. For instance, users and groups let you create accounts and groups on the box. Sources lets you unpack ZIP files. Files lets you create files from inline text or downloaded from a URL. And services, which lets you manage Unix services, such as NTP. Each of these sections has its own unique parameters and behavior. For more information, take a look at the Beanstalk documentation at docs.aws.[amazon.com](https://amazon.com).

### 5. CodeDeploy

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [AWS CodeDeploy key concepts](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-codedeploy-key-concepts?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-codedeploy-key-concepts?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Presenter] We've looked at a few different services that AWS provides for both deployment and provisioning application servers. Next up is CodeDeploy. Unlike Beanstalk, CodeDeploy is not a full [platform as a service](../../Glossary/Concept/PaaS.md). It'll take a bit more setting up, but it provides you with tremendous flexibility to create an end-to-end [DevOps](../../Topics/DevOps.md) pipeline as we'll discover in this chapter. CodeDeploy is designed to give you tools and get out of the way. You write your own deployment scripts using a light framework. CodeDeploy provides hooks for certain deployment events so you can make sure to execute certain actions at the right time. It allows deployment from either S3 or [Git](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Git.md). And finally, unlike Beanstalk, CodeDeploy is a deployment tool only. You'll need to build and provision infrastructure yourself in advance of using CodeDeploy. However, CodeDeploy can deploy to many instances at once as we'll see. There are a few concepts with which you'll need to be familiar before using CodeDeploy. First, the application. As with Beanstalk, you start by defining the app. The application contains a reference to a deployment group. Deployment groups specify which instances CodeDeploy should deploy code to. Applications also specify a deployment configuration, which is a simple directive for how to roll out new code. Options include one instance at a time, half the fleet, or all at once. If you include a load balancer with your installation, you will also have the option for immutable blue-green deployments. From there, you create deployments.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-codedeploy-key-concepts?u=76281980&t=94)** Each deployment represents a push of one application revision to a deployment group. Deployment groups are one of the unique features of CodeDeploy. A deployment group can be defined as any combination of auto-scaling groups, [EC2](../../Skills/DevOps/Amazon%20EC2.md) instances identified by tag values used specify, or even local on-premises non-AWS instances. Local instances must be manually registered with CodeDeploy before they can be added to deployment groups. You have multiple options for deployment sources. App revisions can be defined either by specifying a location in S3 or by supplying the commit ID of a Git revision in a remote Git repository such as [GitHub](../../Skills/Software%20Development/GitHub.md) or [Bitbucket](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Bitbucket.md). This means that, unlike with Beanstalk, you're required to have a remote repository when you want to use CodeDeploy to deploy applications from Git. Whether they're in AWS or on-premises, instances must be configured to work with CodeDeploy by installing the CodeDeploy agent software. AWS Systems Manager gives you an easy way to install this agent by pushing directly to the instance as I'll show later in this chapter. The CodeDeploy agent pulls the AWS CodeDeploy service to check for new jobs. Meaning CodeDeploy doesn't push code rather, the instance agent pulls jobs and executes the deployment itself. Remember: The CodeDeploy does not handle infrastructure for you, so you want to consider some of the many other building and provisioning options such as cloud formation to help. So how does CodeDeploy know how to build your deployment job?
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-codedeploy-key-concepts?u=76281980&t=187)** The appspec.yml file must reside at the root of your app repo. It provides lightweight [Configuration Management](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Configuration%20Management.md) tools, has sections for file movement, permissions, and script executions, and contains hooks for each major CodeDeploy lifecycle event. In the next video, we'll take a look at these lifecycle events in more detail and see an example of this appspec file.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Git](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Git.md) (4), [DevOps](../../Topics/DevOps.md) (1), [Ec2](../../Skills/DevOps/Amazon%20EC2.md) (1), [GitHub](../../Skills/Software%20Development/GitHub.md) (1), [Bitbucket](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Bitbucket.md) (1)
> **CLI Commands:** aws (5), git (4), make (1)
> **Env Vars:** aws (5), ec2 (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2), defined as (1), means that (1)
> **Prerequisites:** you'll need (2), install (1)
> **Cross-References:** later in (1), in the next (1)
> **Tools:** github (1), bitbucket (1)
> **Analogies:** such as (2)

#### [AWS CodeDeploy: AppSpec and lifecycle events](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-codedeploy-appspec-and-lifecycle-events?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-codedeploy-appspec-and-lifecycle-events?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The AppSpec file is the central feature of CodeDeploy. It's the file that lets you tell the service exactly what to do to each host when deploying an app provision. The file itself is written in YAML and must live at the root of your application. Inside, you'll refer to other files and scripts that your host will need to run your app. These scripts will execute at one of five hooks, representing different app lifecycle events. A CodeDeploy app deployment consists of five phases. ApplicationStop, BeforeInstall, AfterInstall, ApplicationStart, and ValidateStatus. At each stage, your appspec.yml may specify scripts for the service to run. These scripts can do anything you'd be able to do if you ran them yourself on the host, so the sky's the limit. CodeDeploy does provide some special sections that wrap common functionality. In addition to the hook section, which let you run arbitrary scripts at those lifecycle phases, you may also specify files and permission sections, which let you move files around and set their permissions. Of course, you could do this with a script invoked via the hook section just as easily, but having these sections makes the file and permission actions a bit more explicit to readers. Soon we'll look at a real appspec.yml file for a simple rubric deployment. For now, take a look at this short example. The version tag at the top is required and will be the same for every appspec.yml file. The file section moves a custom Nginx config to etc/nginx. The source refers to a location in the app provision,
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-codedeploy-appspec-and-lifecycle-events?u=76281980&t=95)** so that means that config file must be in the [GitHub](../../Skills/Software%20Development/GitHub.md) repo or S3 folder if we're using S3 for code storage. Finally, the hook section defines actions for two lifecycle events, BeforeInstall and AfterInstall. You can see that the script invokes two external scripts, also present in the app provision. You can Intuit from the names that they install OS packages and start the web server. These actions will run at the appropriate time during the deploy, thanks to CodeDeploy lifecycle hook support. One final note, when CodeDeploy pulls your revision down to the host, it gets stored at a location under opt/codedeploy-agent/deployment-root, followed by folders for the deployment group and deployment itself, which have long alphanumeric identifiers. It may be that your app's configuration requires you to reference the app path directory, which can be difficult when this path is dynamic like this. Luckily, CodeDeploy provides a few variables in the environment for us to use. Here are all the variables made available to the scripts called by appspec.yml. Lifecycle_Event tells you where you're at in the cycle. Deployment_ID refers to the idea of the deployment that you're currently performing. Application_Name is the AWS name of your CodeDeploy app. Deployment_Group_Name is the readable name of your deployment group. And Deployment_Group_ID is that alphanumeric string of the deployment group. You'll use the two bolded ones here to construct the path to your app provision
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-codedeploy-appspec-and-lifecycle-events?u=76281980&t=189)** when we're deploying.

>

> **CLI Commands:** nginx (2), aws (1)
> **Definitions:** refers to (2), means that (1)

No key pairs necessary. And as for the network, we'll choose now the security group that we just created, the CodeDeploy instance security group. Again, you see how long descriptive names become helpful when you're looking for things that you've created elsewhere. Storage can stay the same as can resource tags. And in fact, actually let's open this up, add a new tag. We'll give it a name tag with the value of, here we go, CodeDeployInstance, we'll just call it CodeDeployInstance. And that way we'll recognize any instances that are created as part of this ASG by their name tag. All right, keep scrolling. Go to advanced details. Very important here. We want to make sure that we include the instance profile that we chose earlier or that we created earlier, the CodeDeployDemoEC2InstanceProfile. All right, so keep going, leave everything else at default. I do want to point out here, by the way, user data. So this is the place where you can give arbitrary script if you need to install packages on the operating system for your application work if you need to do any set of users, this is one good place to do it. >
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-codedeploy-auto-scaling-group?u=76281980&t=279)** but do it when you create the template for the AMI. That way those steps are already done whenever the image is created and they're kind of baked in. Now, if it's something that needs to happen dynamically every time an instance comes up, this would be the place to do it. But we don't need any user data and we're just going to go ahead and click create launch template. All right, we've got our launch template and the next thing we want to do of course is create the Auto Scaling group. And we have a convenient link right here to create Auto Scaling group. If you didn't see this, you can always just go in the menu to Auto Scaling down at the bottom. So we'll click Create Auto Scaling group. Now we'll call this CodeDeployDemoASG for Auto Scaling group. And it's already preselected our launch template 'cause it's the only one that we currently have. And for version, I like to choose latest here. The thing about launch templates is if you make any change to them, they won't take effect until two things happen. One, you associate the Auto Scaling group with the new version of the launch template 'cause you can't just change them as is. Every time you make a change, you have to kind of declare a new version. So if we choose latest here, it means that every time the Auto Scaling group attempts to launch a new instance from this template, it's going to look for the latest version. And so the other thing that has to happen is, a new instance actually has to be created. So different things cause the Auto Scaling group to launch new instances. The template won't be evaluated until it actually launches a new instance. So bear that in mind. If you make a change to the launch template, you just have to prompt somehow,
>
> **[6:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-codedeploy-auto-scaling-group?u=76281980&t=372)** either by deleting an instance and forcing it to create a new one or by increasing the capacity of your Auto Scaling group to get new instances that take on this new template. And you can see some of the options here. You can see, okay, there's the security group that we chose. It's got the the AMI ID, it's got our m1.medium so, all those details are part of the template. Click next and here, go ahead and scroll down. One thing we need to do is choose subnets. So this is where our Auto Scaling group will place the instances that it creates and we'll just select the first couple there. They're across different availability zones as you can see. So that will give this Auto Scaling group a little bit of resilience and your app as well as a result. So click next. At this point, we're not going to create a load bouncer, but we will do that later in this series of videos. So leave it at no load bouncer for now. Leave everything else at default, click next. Desired capacity can stay at one. We're going to keep it at one just for the beginning here and later we'll scale it up. So desired capacity and max capacity can both be at one. Keep on scrolling all the way down, click next. No notification needed, click next. No tags needed, click next and go all the way to the bottom and click Create Auto Scaling group. So at this point we can see the Auto Scaling group here. It's in a status of updating capacity, zero instances right now. So the ASG is doing what it does. It says, okay, our desired capacity is one.
>
> **[7:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-codedeploy-auto-scaling-group?u=76281980&t=465)** We currently have no instances. We need to create one. And we can see that activity going on if we click the name of the Auto Scaling group. Go to the activity tab and scroll down. You can see, all right, we're launching a new EC2 instance. In fact, this has already happened, it's been successful, and that brings up our actual capacity to the desired value of one. And if we want to see that instance, you know, I thought maybe it would give us a link here, but it doesn't seem to, possibly under instance management, we can see that. Like everything else in AWS, IAM rules kind of control everything in terms of permissions. And if our application tried to fetch these secrets through a command through the [CLI](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/CLI.md) or something like that, which we are going to do, if it tried to do that now, it would fail because it has no permissions to read these secrets. And let's take a look inside. There's one statement here that has an allow effect on two actions of Secrets Manager get secret value and describe secret, and these will apply to a particular resource, which is anything that starts with the [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) CodeDeploy. Now, we will copy and paste all this JSON, and then you'll need to edit a few values. So copy that, head back here and paste it in. The first thing is to replace the capital word region with your preferred region, wherever you're building your resources. So for me, that's US East 1, and then the account ID. The account ID is easy to get from this screen. Just go up to your username, click the button and choose the little copy icon here next to your account ID. That'll copy it to the clipboard, highlight account ID, backspace and paste.
>
> **[6:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-codedeploy-managing-secrets-with-secrets-manager?u=76281980&t=377)** And now you should have something that looks kind of like that. And that's all we need for this policy. So just go ahead and scroll all the way down, click next. We'll give the policy a name. We'll call it CodeDeploy Secrets Manager Demo Policy. Again, being very descriptive. You can see the summary here that we've got some read options on Secrets Manager. Very good. Click Create Policy. All right, now with that policy in place and our database up and running, we're ready to go look at our appspec.yaml file and get to a deployment through CodeDeploy.

#### [AWS CodeDeploy: Add a load balancer](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-codedeploy-add-a-load-balancer?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-codedeploy-add-a-load-balancer?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] All right, here we are back at the application. I've added another work of art, 'cause why not, it's fun. And what we want to do now is scale out this application. Let's imagine it's getting very popular, we're getting a lot of traffic, we want more instances to be able to handle that traffic. This is where horizontal scaling comes in, and it's where we're going to be glad that we created this one instance we have in an autoscaling group, 'cause that autoscaling group can be scaled up to have as many instances as we want. Now, once we have multiple instances though, we need to introduce a load balancer. And I've drawn a little picture of what that is going to look like. So here's our one instance and here is the traffic that is currently open. That instance has a public IP and we've allowed port 80 traffic from anywhere in the world to reach it. And what we're going to do is scale up the autoscaling group. So these two are going to belong to the autoscaling group. There'll be a second instance. Now we could just have traffic come to that other instance but it means that our users need to know about two different IP addresses. We obviously don't want that. We want a single point of entry, a single IP or domain name, and that's going to be our load balancer. It's going to take traffic and it's going to route it to one of these two instances so that we start to split our popular traffic across more instances. At that point, we're going to want to change our security groups a little bit because we no longer want this route to be accessible to the world. We want to cut this off and make sure that the load balancer is the only way that users can get in. So we're going to create a security group that allows this to be accessed by port 80, and then change the security group on the instances
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-codedeploy-add-a-load-balancer?u=76281980&t=94)** to allow traffic from the load balancer. And then we'll have that single point of entry. So in order to do that, we need to head back to the AWS console and we'll create the load balancer to start, we'll go to [EC2](../../Skills/DevOps/Amazon%20EC2.md), scroll down to our autoscaling groups, click on the name of our CodeDeployASG, and then scroll down to load balancing. We'll click edit, and here we have the option to choose an existing load balancer, which we don't have. So we're going to choose, add a new load balancer, keep it as an application load balancer, and the default name can stay. Now for this next choice, we want to go with internet facing. Internet facing just means that this load balancer is going to be capable of receiving traffic from the internet. It doesn't mean that it's actually open to it. We'll still need security groups for that, but make it internet facing so that we can set that up later. For the VPC, we have preselected subnets. These are all the same subnets where our ASG is deploying instances, so that matches up. And then we'll have a listener. Similar to internet facing, having the listener here on port 80 doesn't mean that this load balancer is open to that traffic, it just means that it can do it once we put a security group on that will allow that traffic in. Now we need default [Routing](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Routing.md) to go to a new target group. So we'll hit the dropdown, say create target group. A target group is a container. It's literally a target for the load balancer to send traffic to. It's just this sort of logical grouping. And in this case, it will encompass all of the instances that our autoscaling group creates.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-codedeploy-add-a-load-balancer?u=76281980&t=189)** So click update and the load balancer will start to be created. So if you scroll down, you can see it's now here under load balancing, or well, it may be soon. If we go to load balancers, we can see it being created. So it's in a provisioning state and that's going to take a few minutes to come online. So while it does, let's go take care of our security groups. So we're here on the side, we'll go to security groups, and we're going to create a new one. We're going to call it CodeDeployALB for application load balancer. And we'll say allows port 80 traffic from the world. So this will be the route into the ALB. So inbound rules, click add rule, we'll go to HTTP and then allow that from anywhere-IPv4. Okay, so we'll save that security group and then we'll go update our existing security groups. So click security groups again, search for CodeDeploy or filter by CodeDeploy, and we want the instance security group. So if we click on its ID, we'll go edit it. Inbound rules, click edit inbound rules. And you can see we're allowing port 80 from the world. So back to my picture, that's the security group that allows this traffic. So we want to get rid of that rule. So first thing would be to delete that. Again, sorry for being zoomed in. You can see there, delete button.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-codedeploy-add-a-load-balancer?u=76281980&t=283)** Now we want to add a rule, and instead of allowing HTTP traffic in from the world, where do we want to add it from? We want to add it from the load balancer. So we can do that by selecting HTTP, and we don't know the IP of the load balancer, but the good news is we don't have to, we're going to keep this at custom and we're going to type the name of the security group that we're going to attach to the load balancer. So that would be CodeDeployALB that we just created. So as long as we now go and attach that security group to the load balancer, we'll have everything in place. So click save and we'll go check on the load balancer. All right, still provisioning state, so we'll return here when this is available and we'll make sure that we attach the security group to it. All right, just a few seconds later and we've got our load balancer. We're going to go check the checkbox next to it, choose actions, then go to edit security groups, and we'll get rid of the instant security group that we chose earlier and give it the ALB security group. Now we're just about ready, but one thing that we still need to do is head back to our autoscaling group and make sure to actually scale it up. So click into the ASG, here into group details click edit, we'll make our desired capacity 2, and our max desired is already 2, so click update. That will trigger activity on the ASG. You can see it here if you scroll down in activity,
>
> **[6:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-codedeploy-add-a-load-balancer?u=76281980&t=377)** we should momentarily see new activity here that tells us, here we go, that a new instance is coming online because the desired number has changed. All right, so we've got our new instance up and running, and the next thing to do is to go to the load balancer section, make sure the load balancer is highlighted, scroll down all the way on details and copy the DNS name. This is the web address where our load balancer can be reached and the app will be served through it. So over here on this tab, I've loaded up that URL, and you can see that the app does come up. And back here where we were reaching the individual instance, it's no longer reachable. So that's what we wanted. Head back here to the load balancer tab, and if you start to refresh the page, you'll see that we get different answers for the host name. This one ends in an 89, and this one's IP ends in 17. So we are seeing two different instances serve up this application through the load balancer, which is exactly what we wanted. Now at this point, you might be wondering about one last thing. How did our application get onto the second host? After all, the autoscaling group is based on an AMI that doesn't have the app. The AMI only has the CodeDeploy agent, and the application comes from a CodeDeploy deployment. Well, here's where some of the magic of AWS happens. When we set up CodeDeploy with a deployment group pointing to the autoscaling group, it sets some things up behind the scenes, including cues, listeners and hooks and autoscaling so that when autoscaling adds a new instance, it contacts CodeDeploy
>
> **[7:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-codedeploy-add-a-load-balancer?u=76281980&t=471)** and says deploy onto this new instance. So it all takes place without our needing to intervene. And now if we do a new deployment, because we've defined that deployment group in CodeDeploy to include the whole autoscaling group, it will deploy to every instance. Again, remember, based on the pattern that we define, which is, right now, a one-at-a-time deployment, that'll sort of roll through each instance. But if we want anything different, all we need to do is go into CodeDeploy and change that behavior. So now we have achieved true autoscaling. What can you do from here? Well, for one, I would say, look into Amazon's certificate management service so that you can get HTTPS onto this load balancer for secure traffic, and look into redirect rules so that any traffic that comes to HTTP will redirect to HTTPS. And of course, we've got this long domain name here that's not especially friendly to users. That's where Amazon's Route 53 service comes in, where you can bring your own domain name. But we are not done yet. For the next video, we're going to take it up even one more notch, and we're going to add code build support, and then move from there to introducing a code pipeline so that you can have a complete [DevOps](../../Topics/DevOps.md) pipeline where changes will get propagated to your autoscaling group automatically. So I'll see you in the next video.

#### [Troubleshoot AWS CodeDeploy deployments](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/troubleshoot-aws-codedeploy-deployments?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/troubleshoot-aws-codedeploy-deployments?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] CodeDeploy puts a lot of power in your hands but it can be difficult to troubleshoot when things go wrong. Let's look at some common issues you may encounter with deployments. First, instance configuration. Problems with the instances in your deployment group can be the hardest to troubleshoot because your deployment would just time out and you'll not receive an error. Here are some possible causes to keep in mind if that happens. First, the CodeDeploy agent is simply not installed. Remember that each instance used by CodeDeploy must run the service agent. Systems Manager's ability to install and configure the agent via its distributor service is your best friend here. Issue number two, the agent is there but not running. If you install the agent per AWS's instructions, it should be configured as a service that starts on boot. Still, it's something worth checking. Issue number three, S3 access. You might think, "I'm deploying from [GitHub](../../Skills/Software%20Development/GitHub.md). Why do my instances need to access S3?" The reason is that your instances via the agent don't reach out to GitHub directly. Rather, the CodeDeploy service pulls your commit from GitHub then uploads it to S3. The agent then grabs the revision from there in S3. So even when using GitHub, your instance does need access to S3. To confirm the CodeDeploy agent is installed and running, do what we did in the demo. Connect to the instance via SSM or SSH and execute this command. Sudo service codedeploy-agent status. If the agent is running,
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/troubleshoot-aws-codedeploy-deployments?u=76281980&t=93)** you'll get a message that looks like this. Next, issues with the app revision that you're trying to deploy. First, you could have errors in your appspec.yml file. Second, you might have pushed the wrong revision or third, you might have errors in your actual scripts. Let's look at each of these in turn. First, what could go wrong in the AppSpec? Well, it might just be missing from your revision. If you haven't pushed it to your GitHub repository, it won't be there for CodeDeploy to find and use. Second, you might have errors in your YAML syntax. Consider testing your YAML in a validator, many of which can be found on the web. Finally, watch those CodeDeploy hook names. If you misspell or miscapitalize something like AfterInstall, you'll get an error. There are a few reasons the app revision could be wrong. Maybe you gave the wrong commit ID but a more common reason would be that you committed a change but did not actually push the change. Remember that unlike Beanstalk, CodeDeploy actually talks out to GitHub. Finally, script errors. Your deployment scripts referenced by the AppSpec are the most custom part of the deployment so there are any number of reasons they could go wrong. Keep an eye out for insufficient privileges or missing dependencies. The challenges here will vary based on the web framework and programming language that you're using. In fact, you're probably already pretty familiar with how things can go wrong in your stack of choice which makes these locations particularly helpful. You can always view the deployment logs
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/troubleshoot-aws-codedeploy-deployments?u=76281980&t=187)** in the CodeDeploy console but they cut off after a number of lines. If you're at a loss and need to get more information, you can connect directly to one of your failed hosts and check out these file locations where AWS will keep detailed logs of what's going on. All right, let's keep going by adding some [continuous integration](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Continuous%20Integration%20(CI).md) with CodeBuild.

#### [Understanding AWS CodeBuild](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understand-aws-codebuild?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understand-aws-codebuild?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] AWS CodeBuild is a fully managed build service that can build your code, run test, and create output artifacts. It provides a way for [DevOps](../../Topics/DevOps.md) practitioners on AWS to start doing [continuous integration](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Continuous%20Integration%20(CI).md) in the AWS ecosystem. So what is continuous integration exactly? Well, if you're following good development practices, your application already has a suite of automated tests that you run on a regular basis. Each time you make a change to your code, you run your test to make sure that nothing has broken as a result. Running tests locally is fine for solo development. But what if you're on a team? When many people may contribute to the same code base, it's important that testing is performed continuously when anyone merges changes, and that it's done in a central location, not tied to anyone's workstation. Continuous integration provides a feedback loop between you and the [rest](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Representational%20State%20Transfer%20(REST).md) of your team. Builds and tests are triggered on a schedule or in response to something like a [Git](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Git.md) push, and the whole team is notified if something breaks. That's where CodeBuild steps in. CodeBuild is a fully managed pay-as-you-go build service. It's a way to run integration tests on a schedule or by an event. It's a mechanism for generating build artifacts, such as binaries, and distributing them. And it's a tightly integrated component of a [continuous delivery](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Continuous%20Delivery%20(CD).md) pipeline. For example, via AWS CodePipeline. You see, continuous integration is the CI in CI/CD pipeline, continuous integration, continuous delivery.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understand-aws-codebuild?u=76281980&t=95)** Let's look at what happens when CodeBuild executes a build. The job is kicked off by an event, a schedule, a hook on some [Version Control](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Version%20Control.md) repo, or manually, either by you or, say, by CodePipeline. CodeBuild then provisions a runtime environment in which to execute your code. In the case of our ongoing Ruby example, CodeBuild will need to create a Ruby environment that can run the app. You specify the details of this when you create your CodeBuild job. Inside that runtime environment, CodeBuild clones your code from the source repo. It then executes commands provided by you to run the build. These commands will be in a file called buildspec.yaml, which we'll examine momentarily. Finally, CodeBuild can take any output from your job, output CodeBuild calls artifacts, and places them in S3. The result of the whole CodeBuild job is then reported back to the CodeBuild service for you to view. So let's look at that file, the buildspec.yaml. This is the file that drives everything. It resides in the root of an application, can run any command that you need, and contains hooks for running those specific commands at specific points in the build lifecycle. CodeBuild supports four lifecycle hooks, install, pre_build, build, and post_build. Here's an example of what a short buildspec.yaml file might look like. The first line is the version, which is required and doesn't deviate from that bolded text, version: O.2.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understand-aws-codebuild?u=76281980&t=188)** The next section, env, is where you declare environment variables that should exist during the build. In this case, I need RAILS_ENV to be set to the value "test". Additional environment variables would go on additional lines just below. Next, the phases section. This is where I can put commands to execute in each of the build phases I mentioned. In this example, you can see that I've declared blocks of code for two phases, install and build. The command section under each must be succeeded by YAML array of commands that should run in order. In the install phase, I echo out a statement that will appear in the CodeBuild logs and a command bundle install, which installs dependencies from a Ruby Gemfile. Then in the build phase, I issue the rspec command, which actually runs test. Note that in this section, I also use the optional finally block, where I can put commands that will run whether the proceeding command succeeds or not. This gives me a chance to clean up things, if necessary. Finally, artifacts. Any files that are created during your build can be archived by CodeBuild and dropped into an S3 location after the build. This might be some output from your test suite. Or, in the case of a compiled language, maybe it's the actual compilation output. After all, it's called CodeBuild. This process can compile your code, then place it in S3 for later use. If you're making this CodeBuild step part of a CodePipeline deployment, that's a very useful thing to do. So let's take a look at how we'd actually add CodeBuild to our demo app.

#### [AWS CodePipeline challenge: Add an approval stage](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-codepipeline-challenge-add-an-approval-stage?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-codepipeline-challenge-add-an-approval-stage?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] All right, now it's time for another challenge. You've seen how our pipeline works. A [Git](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Git.md) push to the main branch triggers a build, and a successful build kicks off a deployment. That's a great start, but many organizations don't move changes directly from a developer's laptop to production like this. Often there are multiple deployment environments. Something like this is rather common. Your first deployment is to a test environment. This is sometimes called UAT for user acceptance testing, and it's a non-production environment where end users can validate the changes and check that the requirements have been met. It's also a good place to do end-to-end process testing, which can help root out regression errors. Good stuff. When user testing is complete, someone usually has the ability to manually approve the change, releasing it for deployment and production. So for this challenge, let's keep things simple and consider the question, how would we introduce a manual approval stage between the build and deploy stages of this chapter's pipeline? I'll give you a hint. CodePipeline has support for manual approvals built in. You'll just need to explore the UI a bit, maybe search the docs and track down how it works. It will involve using another AWS service to send email messages. Give it a try, and I'll see you in the next video with the solution.

> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Git](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Git.md) (1)
> **CLI Commands:** git (1), aws (1)
> **Env Vars:** uat (1), aws (1)
> **Cross-References:** in the next (1)
> **Documentation:** the docs (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)
> **Non-Speech:** (upbeat music) (1)

#### [AWS CodePipeline challenge solution: Add an approval stage](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-codepipeline-challenge-solution-add-an-approval-stage?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-codepipeline-challenge-solution-add-an-approval-stage?u=76281980&t=0)** (punchy music)
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-codepipeline-challenge-solution-add-an-approval-stage?u=76281980&t=5)** - [Instructor] Now for the solution to this challenge, we're back here at the pipeline. I'm going to click Edit so that we can get into edit mode, which we've been in before. And if you noticed previously, there are these buttons in between the stages, Add stage. So, here between Build and Deploy, this is where we want to make a change. Click Add stage, and we'll name it ManualApproval. Click Add stage. Now, all this does is create a space for us to work, we want to be able to do something between this stage and the last deploy stage. To make it actually do something, click Add action group. Here, we need to give this action a name, and I'll just call it Approval so it looks slightly different from what we just did. When you click on Action provider, you will see a lot of choices. And right here at the top, the solution we've been looking for, Manual approval. If you scroll down, you do get a sense of the depth and breadth of what Code Pipeline can integrate with. We've got [Jenkins](../../Skills/DevOps/Jenkins.md), we've got Cloud Formation, we've got Lambda and Step functions, and many different sources, including [Bitbucket](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Bitbucket.md) and GitLab. So, there's a lot to dive into if you really want to get into the possibilities of Code Pipeline. Choose Manual approval, and we'll see what we get. Now, this first option here is actually optional, so I guess we don't have to do it, but if we provide an SNS topic, we can get an email to a tester whenever there's something to approve. Which is a good thing to do, we want to communicate to our testers, "Hey, a new deployment has reached the UAT environment,
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-codepipeline-challenge-solution-add-an-approval-stage?u=76281980&t=98)** it's time for you to go test and make an approval." So, an SNS topic is something that we have not looked into in this course thus far, and in order to create one, we'll actually need to Cancel here and come back in a moment. SNS stands for Simple Notification Service, and we can get to it by searching SNS here in the top search bar, and opening this in a new tab. In SNS, you create what are called topics. So, here, I don't have any topics yet, so I'm given a shortcut, and we'll call it ApprovalTopic. And the really important thing we need to do is to create a subscription to this topic. So click this button, Create subscription, down below. The protocol is where we will say exactly what form this notification should take. Choose Email. And then for the endpoint, give an email address. Now, I'll give a fake one here, but I've got some pages open where I can show you what this looks like when you actually do it, because you do have to confirm the subscription. > **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-codepipeline-challenge-solution-add-an-approval-stage?u=76281980&t=193)** It's going to look like this, the subscription confirmation email will come to that address, and the user just needs to click Confirm subscription. > **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-codepipeline-challenge-solution-add-an-approval-stage?u=76281980&t=286)** and it looks like this. And that person can click this button to go do the approval. And here's the thing, this button, by itself, does not approve the change, and I feel like this is a little bit of a limitation of this setup, because what it does is it expects you to have a login, and it takes you right back to the pipeline, where you'll see an approval that's about to appear right here. All right, now we have the ManualApproval step here, and we can click Review, where we can approve or reject, and give comments, and then click Submit. With an approval, it will go on to the deployment stage. So, this does require you to come in and click this manual approval, but the thing is, like everything else in AWS, there is a [CLI](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/CLI.md) command to do what I just did. And if you check the AWS docs, you'll see that this can be done remotely. So if you wanted to, for instance, incorporate this into a ticketing system, where maybe you're managing your changes elsewhere, like ServiceNow, or something like that, you could create an integration where someone receives a task in ServiceNow, and clicks approve, and when they do it in that system, then it will trigger off this approval in AWS. So, you can create a whole integration with whatever change or ticketing system you may already have to work with manual approvals in Code Pipeline. So, that is the solution to the challenge,
>
> **[6:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/aws-codepipeline-challenge-solution-add-an-approval-stage?u=76281980&t=382)** and I just want to touch on one more thing. Okay, we talked about this pattern, right? Where you deploy to UAT, manually approve, and then deploy to production. What would it actually take to stand this up? Well, if you think about the steps that we took to stand up the deployment environment in this chapter, we would have a one-time step to just replicate those things. We would create another auto-scaling group, add a load balancer and target groups, add a CodeDeploy project, and then create a production database, although that can be in the same RDS that we're already using. We would introduce new environment variables, probably with Secrets Manager, to tell the production environment where to point to for things like the database. And then finally, we'd have one last CodePipeline stage for the code deploy to production, and we would rearrange it so that the manual approval is before the production deployment. So, with that, if you can do what we've already done in this chapter, you can expand on it to build a truly enterprise-ready deployment pipeline. So, I think that's pretty cool, and I hope you do too. Join me in the next video, where we'll remove the infrastructure we built to make sure that you don't have any unexpected charges.

### 6. Elastic Container Service

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Understanding AWS Elastic Container Service and Fargate](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understand-aws-elastic-container-service-and-fargate?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understand-aws-elastic-container-service-and-fargate?u=76281980&t=0)** - Up until now, we've provisioned, configured, and scaled our web apps with [EC2](../../Skills/DevOps/Amazon%20EC2.md) instances. Full fledged [Virtual Machines](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Virtual%20Machines.md). And while that's a great and effective way to work with AWS, modern web developers know the power of containers to make web workloads even more [agile](../../Skills/DevOps/Agile%20Development.md) and scalable. It's time to talk container orchestration on Elastic Container Service, or ECS. You can find plenty of courses on this site that delve into the world of containers, and if you're looking to deploy one on AWS, you probably already know a good bit, so I'll keep this overview short. Containers are like lightweight virtual machines that share the kernel with the host operating system. Developers can configure them to contain everything an individual app or process needs to run, libraries, binaries, and the like. The result is a very portable image that can be deployed anywhere containers can run, from your own laptop to [Cloud Services](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Cloud%20Services.md) like ECS. One of the great things about Docker images is that they're small enough to be shared. Online container registries like Docker Hub contain thousands of user-defined images with version histories. These images may be used as is or used as the foundational layer for a different application. AWS provides its own Elastic Container Registry, also known as ECR, to facilitate the storage and deployment of containers on AWS. To create a Docker image, you start with a file that describes everything your app needs to run. You build an image from that spec called a Docker file, and then either run it locally or push it to a repository.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understand-aws-elastic-container-service-and-fargate?u=76281980&t=96)** This image depicts the steps we'll soon use to push images to ECR. First, the build, then an authentication step where we log into ECR. We'll then give the image a tag and push it to ECR for later deployment. On ECS, the simplest possible deployment is a task, a single instance of an image. When an image is running, it's known as a container, and you may have as many containers as you want running from a single image source. An ECS task is one container instance of a task definition, which defines everything about how a task should run from the source image, to IAM roles, to security groups. In fact, one of the best things about ECS is its tight integration with AWS concepts like security groups. It makes a very easy transition for folks used to running on EC2. Tasks may have public IPs like an EC2 instance, but are standalone only. They do not scale and they do not automatically restart if they fail. A step up then from a single task is the concept of a service. Unlike a task, services do scale. You can tell a service to keep three copies of the same task alive and if any fails, it will spin up a new container to maintain that scale. It's a common pattern to separate layers of a web application into container services. In this picture, you might have a service that runs many web containers, say our Ruby app, and scales them up, while another service runs a database or a cache or any other supporting service the front end may need. And in that case, maybe you only need one container.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understand-aws-elastic-container-service-and-fargate?u=76281980&t=192)** But if you're wondering, shouldn't I still use AWS or any other available AWS service? You're absolutely right. It's a great choice to combine an ECS layer for your web tier with a service like RDS for your data tier. And we're going to be doing that in our demo. Services can be configured to use a load balancer, making them very similar to EC2 auto-scaling groups. Again, the biggest advantage of ECS is how closely it hews to existing AWS concepts and [Design Patterns](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Design%20Patterns.md). Not only can services use load balancers, but they can be configured for a zero downtime blue/green deployment style where any change you make to the running service actually spins up a copy of that service behind the scenes. Once the copy is up and running, the load balancer cuts over, giving you and your users a seamless change experience. Of course, all this does have to run on infrastructure at some point. That infrastructure layer is called a cluster, and you can run it on EC2 instances using an auto-scaling group, on premises with your own virtual machines, or my preference, using AWS Fargate. With Fargate, AWS fully manages the infrastructure layer, making it so that you have to do zero pre-provisioning whatsoever to get started with ECS. In fact, that's just what we're about to do. In the next video, I'll show you how to run the demo app as a container and we'll see just how simple it can be to deploy and scale it on ECS.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Ec2](../../Skills/DevOps/Amazon%20EC2.md) (5), [Virtual Machines](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Virtual%20Machines.md) (3), [Agile](../../Skills/DevOps/Agile%20Development.md) (1), [Cloud Services](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Cloud%20Services.md) (1), [Design Patterns](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Design%20Patterns.md) (1)
> **Env Vars:** aws (10), ecs (9), ec2 (5), ecr (4), iam (1)
> **CLI Commands:** aws (10), docker (4), make (2), find (1), ruby (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2), known as (2), is called (1)
> **Analogies:** picture (1), similar to (1)
> **Cross-References:** in the next (1)
> **Documentation:** spec (1)
> **Prerequisites:** configure (1)
