Then the .env file name we just created in my_art_gallery. Hit enter and this app will start to run through the container using that environment file. And it's reaching out to our RDS database. So back in your browser, if you head to localhost:3000, making sure to have HTTP on that, then you will see your app come up. So I've already got one image in the database from before
>
> **[9:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/runn-the-demo-app-locally-with-docker?u=76281980&t=562)** and you can see we're running in production, we're running on the Postgres adapter. So the nice thing here is, as long as we establish the network connectivity to the database, and as long as we provide the right environment variables, our app is going to run in ECS just as cleanly as it just ran here on the laptop. The one thing I need you to do is go to the RDS console here and just make sure that you've got the right security groups. Now we just opened up Postgres to the world. We can actually get rid of that now 'cause we will, from here on, be running it through ECS, and make sure that you have the code deployed database security group here and that you still have the CodeDeploy instance security group 'cause we're going to use those two things to have our ECS task talk to the database. If you got Rhythm, just go back to the previous chapter, and create those from the chapter where we added the database to CodeDeploy. So with that, I'm going to modify my database to get rid of that security group, I just get rid of, click apply immediately, go to modify DB instance, and I'll wait for my database to come back online. In the next video we'll kick off this whole ECS deployment by getting the docker image that we just built from our laptop to Elastic Container Registry in AWS.

#### [Run Docker apps with an ECS service](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/run-docker-apps-with-an-ecs-service?u=76281980)

We have a target group with some tasks and later when we want to do a deployment,
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/run-docker-apps-with-an-ecs-service?u=76281980&t=190)** what ECS will do is deploy new tasks and the alternate target group. So you can see how easy it is with ECS to take a docker application running on your local machine, get it into the cloud, and then start scaling it in response to demand. Very cool stuff. #### [Solution: CodeBuild for Docker ECR pushes](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/solution-codebuild-for-docker-ecr-pushes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/solution-codebuild-for-docker-ecr-pushes?u=76281980&t=0)** (upbeat instrumental music)

> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/solution-codebuild-for-docker-ecr-pushes?u=76281980&t=5)** - [Instructor] All right, if you said CodeBuild you're absolutely right. We can automate the entire sequence of steps from [Git](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Git.md) push to Docker build to ECR push using CodeBuild and CodePipeline. And automation is not the only reason to do this. I actually found another good reason, and that is controlling the build environment. I recently started using a Mac with an ARM chip rather than an Intel chip, and by default Docker builds images compatible with the local architecture. So, my ARM-built images weren't working on the Intel-based ECS Fargate. So, one great solution was to get the image building not on my computer but on CodeBuild, so let's see how to do it. We're going to start in the IAM section, so let's head to that service. And we're going to create a new role, so click Roles and Create role. Keep AWS service selected and go down and choose use case, and this will be CodeBuild. Click that option, scroll down and click Next. Okay, so the permission that we're going to give here is basically full access to ECR, the registry. Now, that has a little bit of an older name that we'll have to use in this role. It's [Amazon EC2](../../Skills/DevOps/Amazon%20EC2.md) Container Registry. So, here you can see Container Registry Full Access. > **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/solution-codebuild-for-docker-ecr-pushes?u=76281980&t=190)** and the build process here, or the wizard for CodeBuild, is going to add some additional permissions to that role so that it can also work with CodeBuild. So, that's good. Let's go on down, and here is the all-important buildspec step. All right, let's head once again over to our sample code, and here in the appRoot you'll find a file called buildspec.ecs.yml. Let's take a look at it. All right, so we've got a few phases. Here in the install phase we've got three lines that just makes sure that the AWS [CLI](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/CLI.md) is installed and up to date. We also output the version just so you can see that in the logs. We've got echo statements peppered throughout that will let you, again, see in the logs what is happening and what stage you're at. Here we set up two environment variables, REPOSITORY_URI and DEFAULT_REGION. You will definitely need to update the repository URI, and you may need to update the region to reflect the one that you've been using. Here you'll recognize the ECR get-login command that pipes in to Docker login. So, this establishes a connection to ECR through Docker. Here in the build phase we echo out a few more things, and we do the local Docker build using, again, the dot that indicates that Docker build should look for a Docker file in the current folder. We tag that image using this tag right here. Because we've got this environment variable we need a backslash to escape the colon, and the tag will be latest. Here in post_build we echo a few more things out, and then we push that tag to ECR.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/solution-codebuild-for-docker-ecr-pushes?u=76281980&t=286)** Now, the final part here is new, and I want to point out what it's doing. We need two artifacts to come out of this build stage so that later we can automate this pipeline fully. The first one is a file called imageDetail.[JSON](../../Skills/Web%20Development/JSON.md). It needs to have that name and have this format, ImageURI, and then we give it the fully qualified path to the URI and tag. So, that's the same tag that we pushed earlier, and it's going to go into imageDetail.json. Then, here in the appRoot, you can look, there's a file called appspec.ecs.yml. This is an alternate app spec file for use with CodeDeploy that we use in a video or two here to actually fully automate this and deploy through the CodePipeline that we're now building. To do that we'll need it to be an appspec.yml file that is accessible as an artifact. So, these two files, by virtue of living here in the artifact section, will be something we can use later in the pipeline. Okay, now what I want you to do is copy this whole buildspec.yml, go back and copy that into your CodeBuild. Click Switch to editor, erase what's here by default and paste in. Now, we need to replace the URI for our repository. So, go up to the search bar, type ECR, and open this in a new tab by right-clicking or Command + clicking. And we can grab the URI here with this Copy button. So, go back to the first tab
>
> **[6:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/solution-codebuild-for-docker-ecr-pushes?u=76281980&t=380)** where you're still editing the CodeBuild, highlight that, and paste over your URI. Okay, at this point we're done with the buildspec so scroll down. Here under CloudWatch let's fill in the name BuildMyArtGallery so we can get logs. Scroll all the way down and click Create build project. From here the project is created, and we can click Start build. In a few moments this will finish and we can look at the logs and see that our tag has been pushed to ECR. Okay, a few minutes later and the task has succeeded. And if you scroll down you can see all the logs of what just happened. Let's go to the Elastic Container Registry, and we can see here in our repository we've got the latest image with a recent timestamp that lets us know it has been overwritten. But you can see here in the deployment status, we're done. The replacement task set is in place. The traffic has been rerouted. If we go to our second tab here, we can refresh, and there is our subtitle. We've actually made the deployment already. The only thing that's left to do is wait that five minutes that we configured to take the old task out of commission. So this is pretty cool. We have completely automated from start to finish a Docker deployment on ECS. It's fully scalable. We can set it to whatever number of tasks we want. We can kick off a deployment with a git push. You know, you add your collaborators in there. You start working with pull requests. You get code reviews going. Those merges are going to kick off deployments. You've got everything you need here to start building up a really sophisticated, collaborative CI/CD environment where your changes can be deployed to production in just a matter of minutes. Very, very cool stuff with AWS. So, congratulations on making it this far. I hope that you find a lot of things valuable here and have some ideas of how to build on this. But in the next video, we will go ahead and tear it all down so we make sure that you don't get charged anything that you don't intend to. So we'll see you in the next video.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>

#### [Understand AWS Copilot](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understand-ecs-copilot?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understand-ecs-copilot?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Hey, just a quick note before we completely wrap up with containers. If you've enjoyed what we've been able to do with ECS in this chapter, be sure to check out AWS [Copilot](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Copilot.md), not to be confused with AI services of the same name. Copilot is Amazon's brand for the latest version of its ECS Command Line Interface. As you can see here, Copilot has its own unique landing page and documentation at aws.[github.io](https://github.io), and looking at the usage message here on the terminal with its playful emojis, you can guess that Copilot is designed to give developers a friendly experience complete with step-by-step wizards that help developers rapidly standup ECS services. The key concepts in Copilot are the app, environment, and services. When you start a new build with Copilot in it, you'll be asked a series of questions that'll help you launch a scalable container infrastructure in minutes. One of the things I really like about Copilot is that it generates config files for you that manage things like network infrastructure, security groups, and task scaling on a per environment basis, giving you an easy way not only to configure your environment, but to [Version Control](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Version%20Control.md) that infrastructure. You can see here we've got references to security groups, and down below to secrets. In fact, that's another great feature of Copilot, the ability to easily push App Secrets to Secrets Manager with the Secret command. And don't miss this Pipeline command, which will help you quickly stand up the same kind of pipeline we just built in the web console with Copipeline, all from the terminal. To demo Copilot fully would require several more videos,
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-software-deployment/understand-ecs-copilot?u=76281980&t=96)** but do give it a try. When you see it kick off and build all your infrastructure and resources in one fell swoop, I think you'll agree that ECS Copilot is both a fun and powerful tool for working with ECS.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Copilot](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Copilot.md) (10), [GitHub](../../Skills/Software%20Development/GitHub.md) (1), [Version Control](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Version%20Control.md) (1)
> **Env Vars:** ecs (5), aws (1)
> **Tools:** terminal (2), command line (1), github (1)
> **CLI Commands:** aws (2)
> **URLs:** [github.io](https://github.io) (1)

For more on these and many other topics, head to the documentation@docs.aws.[amazon.com](https://amazon.com), or check out the many other excellent AWS courses in this site's library.
